## Thread: OT: A warning — 6 this month / 40 total

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: A warning

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-01T11:09:43-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kbv1p802q0t@news6.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<ak466jFi2qkU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<aji34mFjh1aU1@mid.individual.net> <ajj7psFr1dvU1@mid.individual.net> <kb1q5v$20g$1@reader1.panix.com> <ajk96gF4ao9U1@mid.individual.net> <f_adnb3K_qroM0TNnZ2dnUVZ_tmdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fgejd89io1kula463tu1i0qd1in0e3tnic@4ax.com> <ajv2r5FephiU1@mid.individual.net> <kbf6pq01ris@news1.newsguy.com> <ak1acuFtgikU1@mid.individual.net> <kbhrbk0120n@news1.newsguy.com> <ak466jFi2qkU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> Are you doing formal research into malware?

On occasion. It's not a primary part of my job, but once in a while I 
develop simple exploits as proofs-of-concept or to test defenses. I 
follow some security lists (Buqtraq, RISKS, etc) and read some of the 
technical papers in the area.

 > Is your "threat model" something
> we could read/review or is it a private project?

I don't have anything written down. I only manage my own personal 
machines these days, so I haven't had to formalize it. Back when I had 
a suite of development Unix boxes to look after, and a subordinate to 
supervise, I did have a high-level security plan, which had a threat 
model, but it was pretty specific to that set of systems.

I wrote the threat model for the system security features in one of 
the Micro Focus product lines, but again that's a pretty specific 
model; if you're not doing CICS emulation it's probably not of much 
interest. Also, it requires other defenses - the underlying axiom of 
that threat model is "do what you need to do to trust your developers, 
because this security feature isn't going to protect you from them".

So my informal threat model for my handful of development machines is 
basically a list of heuristics I keep in my head, like:

- If basic counter-intrusion mechanisms are used properly, the most 
probable source of risk is data loss.

- The most likely sources of data loss, roughly in order, are human 
error, hardware failure (incidental or due to external causes such as 
power strikes), malicious behavior by insiders, theft of equipment in 
public spaces, and malicious behavior by outsiders. That seems to be 
supported by research I've seen, and correlates with my own 
experience. Somewhere in that list I also need "unavailable personnel" 
(the "I perish in an unanticipated heroic world-saving act and no one 
else knows my password" threat), but I'm not sure precisely where it 
should be.

- Basic counter-intrusion mechanisms begin with five major 
foundational items: the principle of least privilege (which starts 
with "don't do things with administrative privilege if they don't 
require it"), sensible user practices, applying security fixes in a 
robust fashion, staying educated about current major threats, and 
defense-in-depth with basic anti-intrusion and anti-malware software. 
That last item is necessary but by no means sufficient.

- Protection against data loss boils down to multiple forms of backup, 
including versioning. High-value data that changes frequently in 
particular needs strong versioning, with the ability to retrieve old 
versions, track changes, and compare versions; for developers, that 
means source code control. I put most of the files I touch under 
revision control of some sort. Beyond that, I have various kinds of 
on- and off-site backup.

- For loss-of-personnel, I have a password list locked in my home 
safe. That's really a poor solution but I haven't come up with a 
better one yet.

- Loss of data *availability* is a secondary risk. Data that's not too 
sensitive is backed up to Amazon S3, so I have access to it from 
anywhere with an Internet connection; it's protected by access rules 
and encryption.

- After building a threat model (even an informal one like that) and 
implementing the risk-mitigation steps it supports, you still need to 
account for the possibility of loss. So you have to have some idea 
about a recovery plan.

> Can it be summarised into simple plain advice for people?

I don't know that the above is simple or plain, and that's already 
pretty abbreviated. This is one of the great problems of information 
security. The flexibility of data processing means data is exposed to 
all sorts of threats, and it's hard to account for all of them.

> I can say that one of the things I have learned out of this is that 
> partition copies are a very useful recovery mechanism. I had previously 
> thought more in terms of backing up critical data (and I still do that) but 
> if the whole world goes to custard and you can't even boot, a working 
> partition image is a blessed relief.

Yes. Recovery partitions and/or external boot media are tremendously 
helpful. Unfortunately it can be difficult keeping a full partition 
copy on portable equipment.

> Despite having worked with PCs since they were invented, I have never had a 
> disk crash (touch wood), and in twenty years before that I never saw a head 
> crash on a mainframe either. I believe such events are extremely rare, 
> although, of course, they can and do occur.

I imagine it depends quite a bit on the equipment in question and 
environmental conditions. I've had three laptop hard drives fail 
spontaneously and completely. The first one froze - it was in an 
office I rented and while I was away over a holiday the heat failed. 
Sometimes hard drives can recover from that sort of thing; sometimes 
they can't. The other two laptop drive failures were both 
controller-logic failures, one total (drive wouldn't even spin up) and 
the other partial (gradually increasing numbers of errors). Both were 
Hitachi Travelstar drives. The totally-failing one failed, ironically, 
as I was doing a periodic backup of email archives, just prior to 
leaving for a three-week trip. (MF had to overnight a temporary 
replacement laptop to me.)

I've had a couple of consumer-grade desktop SCSI drives, from the 
early '90s, fail. They were kept under less than optimal conditions, 
in white-box enclosures, and may have been running outside their 
recommended heat envelope.

I've seen a *lot* more data loss (mostly recoverable, thank goodness) 
due to human error, though. Once I saw a co-worker do a global delete 
of all the batch files on a Windows 3.x development system - he meant 
to type "*.bak", typed "*.bat" by mistake, and hit Enter just as I 
said "wait!". Well, several hours of undelete later, we had a plan to 
immediately institute routine backups of all the development machines...

> I learned a long time ago to look at MTBF figures from manufacturers of disk 
> drives (I used to work for one, many years ago) and work out how long it 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> useful, that's all...)

Agreed.

> I don't run my disk 24/7. In terms of ACTUAL time in use it is probably 
> around 15/7 or  5478.75 hours per year. So, on average (mean, actually....) 
> I can expect  30,000 / 5478.75 = 5.47 years of use.

Most of mine are on 24/7, since they do off-hours scans and backups. I 
should probably look into tuning those schedules so the machines can 
sleep more often; it'd also slow down how fast they accumulate dust 
and whatnot.

> I replace most hard disks every 4 years for this reason.
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> So far, I've never had a crash...

Sounds like a good plan. I may try instituting that myself.

With SMART-enabled drives in theory there should be decent probability 
of being warned before serious failures, but I have to admit that I've 
never had an actual SMART warning on any of my equipment.
```

- **From:** shakirvohra <mushtaq_shakir@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-03T23:36:35-06:00

- **Message-ID:** `<PrednT_U29f-93vNnZ2dnUVZ_vGdnZ2d@giganews.com>`

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Micro Focus Software 4 Sale

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-04T20:22:32+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<akne9qFpbqvU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<wPiYb.52242$IF1.7452@nwrdny03.gnilink.net> <PrednT_U29f-93vNnZ2dnUVZ_vGdnZ2d@giganews.com>`

```
shakirvohra wrote:
> any one want to sell cobol netexpress 5.1 please?

If you are prepared to reinstall it every 30 days you could use the free 
version. (you need a virtual machine, but if you only use it occasionally, 
that's fine. You can download free virtualization software from Sun 
VirtualBox (recommended) or VMWare... both excellent products.)

Speaking as someone who has used it, I can tell you that the NetExpress 5.1 
implementation is of no interest to Micro Focus,who cannot/will not support 
it, you will need to pay a runtime license fee on anything you develop with 
it, and if you want to develop COM components it is "best avoided". 
(Although it technically supports them, the process for doing so is 
unecessarily convoluted, all COM components have to be developed through a 
Project, they require "trigger files" for registration, and there is no 
facility to batch compile such components.) For straightforward procedural 
COBOL 85 it is adequate, but Fujitsu is a better option for small developers 
because there is no run time fee.

Mocro Focus are more focused on their more recent offerings, and you cannot 
really blame them for not being enthusiastic supporters of NE 5.1.

Both Micro Focus and Fujitsu implementations of COBOL (non CLI generating 
versions... i.e. native code implementations... Say NE 5.1, Fujitsu Netcobol 
version 6.1 through version 9) are very good as far as COBOL goes, but they 
are both expensive (even now, when they have all been "replaced" by CLI 
generating versions for .Net), and, as far as I can tell, it is no longer 
possible to buy a single seat license for any of these products. (I was 
quoted $US20,000 for a 5 seat (minimum) license for Fujitsu Version 10, and 
maintenance is mandatory, not optional.)

This is just ludicrous for a product which has been superseded for years 
now; no wonder COBOL is dying.

I would suggest you try e-bay to see if someone who has moved on from COBOL 
will sell you their licensed copy of NE 5.1.

Personally, I wouldn't buy it at any price... :-)

I will sell a licensed copy of Fujitsu NetCOBOL, equivalent to NE 5.1 but 
without the runtime fees, (which is a very good native code implementation 
of COBOL, including support for COBOL '85 plus extensions, OO COBOL and 
PowerCOBOL for quickbuild GUI applications, plus mainframe flagging, but I 
only have a very limited number of licenses remaining, and maintenance is 
not included (although I will provide reasonable support to anyone who 
purchases), if you contact me privately. These are second hand transfers of 
ownership and not new products.

Pete.
```

## Thread: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens? — 89 this month / 91 total

### What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2013-01-04T22:59:57-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Here is an article on the subject:

http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=why-touch-screens-will-not-take-over&WT.mc_id=SA_CAT_BS_20130104
```

#### ↳ Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-06T00:05:12+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> Here is an article on the subject:
>
> http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=why-touch-screens-will-not-take-over&WT.mc_id=SA_CAT_BS_20130104

An interesting article but the authoor is firmly stuck in "here-and-now". 
Yes, if you repetitively have to move your arm to touch a screen, you may 
get "gorilla arm", but that is not the goal of the "Touch screen" 
technology.

The "touch screen" won't be a screen; it will be ANY surface. Your hand, an 
exercise book, the wall, your car windscreen... any surface close to you.

Touch screens are just the beginning.

Microsoft are betting on us becoming completely interactive with devices, to 
the point where, after a while, we won't even see the device; just the 
interaction. And that bet is not just a few million spare dollars hanging 
around, it is BILLIONS...

It will be a while yet and some of us here probably won't get to see it 
become as everyday as mobile phones and the Internet, but my personal 
opinion is that it will.

The mindset regarding computers and the use of them that most people here 
hold, is NOT the mindset of the rising generation. These people never saw a 
mainframe, they never learned to program in COBOL, they are only interested 
in functionality. If they can do it on a mobile phone, then how could they 
NOT do it on a "computer"?

Here's a different viewpoint from the one expressed in the article:

http://www.pcworld.com/article/2020268/meet-microsoft-the-worlds-best-kept-randd-secret.html

The OmniTouch rig is a prototype. When it is produced it is likely to be a 
tiny device that can be pinned to your shirt like a button or brooch. If you 
think about Web Cams when they were first developed they were fairly large 
devices that clipped to your notebook screen. Nowadays they are buried as a 
single spot in the frame of most notebook screens. It is a similar 
evolution.

Take the trouble to view some of the videos referenced in the article and 
you will see what I am talking about.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-06T09:47:06+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<akrhqbFn604U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net> <1rGdna5yTMPb7XXNnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net...
…[59 more quoted lines elided]…
> interface?"

Did you follow the link?

No comments?
(Just want to check I am not wasting my time by responding...)

As it is you, Charlie, I'll respond to your last question anyway.

I am using Windows 8 and find it to be the best OS that Microsoft has ever 
produced.

The "touch screen interface" is actually implemented as a "touch or click 
interface" and is no problem at all.

There has been some fierce debate (and I have been involved in some of it) 
in other forums where people have largely bleated about the loss of the 
START button, without realizing that the whole START screen replaces this 
button and you can configure it to suit yourself very easily, on the fly.

The Windows desktop has not gone away, it is just not the first thing you 
see. (You COULD configure things so it IS the first thing you see but that 
would be a stupid thing to do; it would be like buying a Ferrari then 
chopping the roof off because you are pining for your VW convertible...) The 
desktop is a press of the Windows button away. (Or you can use the "touch 
interface" to get to it.)

If you don't like tiles and apps (and they are a taste which I am slowly 
acquiring) then don't use them. You could stick with only using the Desktop 
and STILL enjoy a quantum leap in performance and reliability. (Cold boot in 
under twenty seconds, resume from Hibernation in under 5 seconds... plus 
much more seamless updates and resource management). I'm gradually coming 
round to having the news and weather and things like Kindle and an "All 
Blacks" tile at my fingertips, but I still spend most of my time in the 
Desktop.

I have no interest whatsoever in Facebook or social media so that part of it 
doesn't affect me. I DID go and retrieve the Windows Chess game which is not 
provided with Win 8 but that's about it as far as games go and I had a look 
through the games store but things like Angry Birds just don't ring my bell. 
(Millions of people enjoy it and, as I have never played it, I am not 
qualified to judge.)

Most of the criticism is from people who fiercely resist change. I find it 
staggering that people who you would reasonably expect to be innovators and 
early adopters are, in fact, the exact opposite.

There is nothing "hard" about Windows 8. Newbies to computing take to it 
like ducks to water; kids who have mobile phones just use it intuitively. I 
know a 7 year old who is showing her parents how to get around from Metro to 
Desktop and uses a wireless mouse as if it were a touch screen. (In fact, I 
learned an app shortcut from her the other day which I didn't know, (Alt/F4 
to close an app) and she had learned it from an older kid at school.

If you really can't cope with change to the Windows environment then your 
alternatives are Mac OS or Linux.

Start over with a clean slate.

Good luck!

Pete.
```

```
>you indicated the the desktop is still available.  Thanks for your input. 


As Pete said, you don't actually have to use much of the touch stuff
in Win8, especially on a desktop machine with a mouse.  In fact, it
runs just fine with the vast majority of existing desktop monitors
which don't have touch support at all, and you can do most of the
"touch" actions with a mouse.  And as Pete said, the old style desktop
(or an approximation thereof) is at worst a couple of clicks away.

The front page *does* look shockingly unfamiliar at first, but it's
really not a big deal.  The switching between apps with a Metro-style
UI and "conventional" style apps can be a bit jarring.  And your Win8
desktop experience will definitely be better if you learn a few of the
keyboard shortcuts.

You should definitely check it out at your local store.  And if you
hate it, machines with Win7 installed instead are pretty easily
available.  At the end of the day, WinXP is not long for this world.
```

```
On Jan 6, 9:47 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> Most of the criticism is from people who fiercely resist change. I find it
> staggering that people who you would reasonably expect to be innovators and
> early adopters are, in fact, the exact opposite.

That sounds like dogma to me. Just because Microsoft want to force not-
Metro down everyone's throats until they _demand_ it on their tablets
and phones (actually they phrased it as 'making it the most familiar
UI so users would demand it on ..') does not mean that people who
criticize it are Luddites.

There seem to be many valid reasons for not liking not-Metro, one is
the distraction caused by the jarring switch between the familiar
desktop and the tiled start screen. Another is that the 'active tiles'
are distracting, like banners and animated gifs on web-sites. The
previous iteration of active desktop in Windows 98 was rejected by
most users for the same reason. But the main reason given is not that
it is change in itself, but because it _reduces_ productivity.

Not-Metro may be excellent on a phone for those who want to keep
connected all the time with facebook, twitter and the rest, though it
was firmly rejected on Kin and WP7/8 sales are still declining, but it
just doesn't seem useful for most of those who use computers as a work
tool. I don't even like the Windows 95 UI style of having icons all
over the desktop, I never see my 'desktop' because I have several
overlapping application windows that permanently cover it.

As for touch on the desktop it is a complete non-starter. I work with
my feet up, keyboard on knee and my monitors are not even within
reach. I do occasionally use an on-screen keyboard and click that with
the mouse for short interactions when I can't be bothered to reach for
the keyboard after I have put it back on the desk. Reaching for the
monitor is a complete non-starter.

I have, however, developed a system that was designed for a desktop
sized touch screen monitor. This is for use in a workshop where
workers record their starting and finishing of jobs. It is web based
and is quite easy to do in HTML, JavaScript and AJAX. It is equally
usable with a mouse. They walk up, touch the various controls and to
close and open jobs and go back to their machines, or have a touch
screen, even an iPad, at their station.

With finger smearing on a phone this is easily dealt with by wiping it
on your clothing. Not so easy for desktop monitors, I hate it when non-
touch monitors show finger marks as users point out item to others.

As for gesturing in the air or using other 'virtual' inputs this will
go the way of voice input - useful is very rare circumstances and
rejected by most users. I don't want my waving goodbye to someone to
wipe half my computer work, or the office fool to disrupt my
programming by throwing stuff at my desk.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is your opinion regarding touch screens?

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@att.net>
- **Date:** 2013-01-05T16:19:56-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1a2dnaJuxsuUOnXNnZ2dnUVZ5radnZ2d@giganews.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<1rGdna5yTMPb7XXNnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net> <1rGdna5yTMPb7XXNnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
On 1/5/2013 12:26 PM, Charles Hottel wrote:
> (snip)
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I bought a new Win7 desktop last August rather to avoid upgrading from 
32bit WinXP to 64bit Win8.  It turns out 64bit Win7 broke some of my old 
apps anyway.  Most early Win8 PC's do not have touchscreens, but they 
will work fine with a mouse and keyboard.

My only experience with Windows 8 is demo models in a store.  You should 
be able to press the "windows" key to toggle between the tile interface 
and a desktop that looks almost exactly like Win7 (but without a start 
button).  Depending on where you start from, you might need to press the 
windows key and then the "D" letter key (Win+D) to navigate from the 
tile interface to the desktop.

If you miss the Start button there is a free download that will add one 
for you:
http://classicshell.sourceforge.net/

Classic Shell can be configured to look like either a WinXP or a Win7 
start button.  If you prefer the WinXP start button on Win7, you can do 
that with Classic Shell.  There are other Start button add-ons 
available, mostly for free:
http://lee-soft.com/vistart/index.html
http://www.startbutton8.com/index.html
http://retroui.com/     ($4.99 for personal use)

A quick google search should find Windows 8 keyboard shortcuts.  Here 
are some of the most useful Win8 keyboard shortcuts:

Windows-C Open the Charms bar (sort of a start menu replacement)
Windows-D Show the Desktop
Windows-E Open File Explorer (formerly Windows Explorer)
Windows-F Go to Files in the Search charm
Windows-H Go to the Share charm
Windows-I Go to the Settings charm
Windows-K Go to the Devices charm
Windows-L Lock the device
Windows-M Minimize all windows (only on the Desktop)
Windows-Q Go to Apps in the Search charm
Windows-R Launch the Run box
Windows-W Go to Settings in the Search charm
Windows-X Open a menu of tools for power users
Windows-Z Launch the App bar (or make it disappear if it's already showing)
Windows-Home Minimize non-active Desktop apps
Windows-up arrow Maximize a Desktop app
Windows-down arrow Restore/minimize a Desktop app
Windows-F1 Run Windows Help and Support

You can still buy new PC's with Win7 installed, but they are getting 
harder to find.  If you like to build your own PC, you can still get 
WinXP or Win7.  If you want to dual boot between Windows and Linux, try 
to stay with Win7, because UEFI makes it very difficult to install Linux 
on a Win8 PC.

Kind regards,
```

```
I'm with you Charles.  Touch screens are fine for smart phones and

the touchpad and if you happen to let your thumb drop only Bill Gates
know where your cursor ends up.  I have found in Win XP that you can
disable the touchpad if the PC senses a mouse and that seems to work
fine.
```

```
On Tue, 8 Jan 2013 12:05:27 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>> much snipped
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>support but it will be more...

I purchased the download of Windows 8 for my 2 gigabyte Acer One 722
netbook from Microsoft online.  If it was any other company than
Microsoft I would have stopped the transaction because I had to enter
my credit card details before told me that I had paid 39.99 Canadian
plus 6.00 Harmonized Sales Tax.  I suspect the netbook I bought in
March 2012 was basically the same as the Win 8 version except for
UEFI.  I had bought it for e-mail, web browsing and word processing
while traveling and have been pleasantly surprised by the good
performance of it.  Windows 8 Professional has not changed that
performance by much.  

Since both my wife and I like Firefox we are using it from the desktop
and I have not signed up for a Windows account and given the purchase
experience I am reluctant to give Microsoft any credit card
information.  I also don't want to keep my calendar on Skydrive.  Some
of the apps look like they might be interesting but I haven't had the
time to play with them and I really don't want bandwidth and cpu taken
up with streaming items that are for me a distraction.   I find Win 8
annoying but not a reason to rush out and buy a Windows 7 laptop as a
replacement for my wife's XP computer.  For example, to shutdown I
shouldn't have to swipe to bring up charms, scroll down to settings
and click on it, click on power, then click on shutdown.  There should
be an easy way to get to the programs that are not on my desktop.  I
am grateful that I put run on my desktop because that is what my wife
and I use to run Agent (for our e-mail) off our respective USB keys.
As I understand it from one of the Windows 8 magazines IE 10 has to be
invoked in the desktop mode to make some of the configuration changes
which is idiotic.  Rather than having to have all of the tiles on the
main screen, there should be the ability for group tiles (a feature of
Android 4.0 on phones) which get you a family of tiles.  

I botched the upgrade somewhat because I should have applied the ACER
updates before upgrading to Win 8 and I still have one ACER related
update to do.  Norton 360 is working OK although Win 8 is unhappy
because the Windows firewall is turned off.  Windows update is harder
to get to and use which is really aggravating when applying the myriad
of fixes when doing the initial upgrade.  I also found I had to apply
at least one set of fixes individually because they would not go on as
a group which was really aggravating and I think I also had to do some
contortion or other when that group of fixes failed.  I don't believe
in auto-update.  I will buy another Win 8 upgrade license if I am
allowed to defer the actual upgrade to Windows 8 Pro for up to a year.
I like my desktop (still on Win 7) with my 23 inch screen and would be
interested in an appropriate device to use with it (touch really
doesn't work on a desktop).   However as I age and my fine motor which
has never been more than average at best, I suspect touch screens and
devices will become more problematic.  I know that I find my Motorola
Pro Android 2.3.1 phone is subject to mistouching and can be annoying
when scrolling though the various tiles to get to the few that I use.
While I can see and agree with the idea of a unified approach across
devices, I am less than impressed with the current implementation.  

Clark Morris
```

```
Based on your experience and my prior experience, I will be looking at
alternatives next month prior to renewal time.  
>
>> Windows update is harder
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>
For the netbook especially, I find it far safer to be notified of
pending updates and choosing when to download and install. Shutting
down the computer during an update process doesn't seem overly
brilliant.  I also don't like unexpected extended shutdowns.  Normally
I do the updates either at time of notification or within a day of
that notification unless I am on the road.  Even then I get antsy
about not being up to date.  All of the computers in the house are
shut down nightly and the netbook is not always on.  Given that the
primary use is as a travel computer it can be off for days.  In this
instance, I ran into the problem as a part of post processing for the
upgrade.  I suspect I might have been in worse shape with the normal
auto update process because it seems to have been related to an
abnormal case of not being able to apply all of a given group of
updates before restart.  Because I was monitoring the process, I knew
what had happened and was able to take corrective action.  So far as I
am concerned the update is friendlier on 7 than on 8.  On 7 I am able
to review all critical and optional updates at the same time (I don't
think that I have ever left out a critical update) and then apply all
critical plus selected optional updates.  My wife's laptop is set up
to do automatic updates.  I also run Secunia's Personal Security
Inspector to get notifications of other software updates and my
Internet Security products always have been on auto update.  
>
>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>Get a refund, stick with Win 7, be happy... :-)

Why?  I'm not going back and I'm assuming that Microsoft will make new
world worth having.  I will use Win 8 on the netbook and play with it
on the computer that isn't critical in the house (this may be changing
because my wife is using it to play CBC Radio 2 (Music) from the
Internet through our stereo system.  For 45.99 CAD I would like to
purchase the right to upgrade my Win 7 desktop later this year after
some of the kinks have been worked out.

Clark Morris
>
>Pete.
```

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Wed, 9 Jan 2013 23:59:19 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> something Walter Mossberg found out the hard way.

I'm trying really hard not comment on Win 8 because I don't want to become a 
free Help desk for it and I really don't care whether other people like it 
or not, but as it is you Clark, I'll respond.

First off, Mossberg was an idiot. A piece of excellent software is provided 
(just as it was for Windows 7) which will analyse your existing 
configuration, including hardware and drivers, and will tell you quite 
specifically whether you can upgrade without problem or not.

He didn't even run it, then bleats about having problems.

For myself, I ran it before even thinking about upgrading to Win 8. It gave 
the target machine a clean bill of health and it installed in 10 minutes 
with no problem whatsoever. Neither have I had any trouble since. Updates 
are much more efficient and unobtrusive (I actually allow Win 8 to download 
them without telling me, which I have never done before; I can see if 
updates were installed if I need to know, but, so far, it just works like 
clockwork and everything runs.)

If you prefer to take the advice of a computer publication against the 
advice of a Microsoft program which has actually run on your system, then 
that is a matter for each of us; I don't think it is fair to blame the 
magazine for not knowing that someone has an incompatible device in their 
configuration, although they should certainly advise people to use the 
upgrade checker and be sure.



> Getting this
> information from a vendor about a computer that is over a year old is
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> only have one of them there.

Try it and see. You may be surprised. There are principles here. If you can 
pin anything executable to the Start screen (and I believe you can...) then 
why would you not be able to pin several Browsers?  It's no good just 
believing what they write; weigh it against what you know.


>
> I skipped the intro to Windows 8 when I installed the upgrade and
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> can easily put USB 3.0 ports on this and also safely put on Win 8.

That sounds sensible to me. If someone has already blazed the trail, use 
their experience.

However, in the final analysis there is no substitute for simply trying 
stuff. If it works, it trumps anything that anybody has written to the 
contrary.

Pete.
```

```
The article referenced below is giving me pause in my consideration
for getting a license to run Win 8 on my 2010 Acer M5811 desktop with
8 gigabytes of memory, a potent video card and quad core Intel.  The
compute may have been a new design in 2009.  The computers in the
article date back to 2005 and 2009 and in both cases the manufacturers
did not test or warrant the computers for Windows 8.  Acer does not
list the M5811 as a model for which there are software upgrades.  The
Windows 8 checker utility does not flag that much as being
incompatible but it is hard to say.  In the article, Walter Mossberg
admits he did inadequate preliminary checking for which he probably is
embarrassed since he is a knowledgeable technology columnist for the
Wall Street Journal.  I will check my video drivers (and card) as well
as my hard drive.  

In one sense, it may come down to the cost of replacing the box as
opposed to the cost adding USB 3.0 ports and Win 8 to my current
machine.  Windows 8 still looks like a good way to ease into new world
despite what I consider some dubious or bad implementation decisions. 
Clark Morris

For forwarding..
```

```
I've checked the ACER web-site and have run the advisor on both my

ACER lists the netbook as being upgradeable while the far more
powerful desktop is not mentioned.  The hope for my desktop is that
none of the problematic hardware he mentioned (touchscreen and
multi-gesture trackpad) is on my desktop.  The fun is that a machine
which on the face of it seems that it could handle Windows 8 may not
be able to because of driver problems.  That is why anyone who is
looking to upgrade a machine NOT listed on the manufacturer's web-site
for upgrade to Windows 8 needs to do careful research.  Because of the
potential I see in Windows 8 as opposed to some of the implementation
decisions that aggravate me, I will be doing the research.  I might
add that the latest batch of 11 critical updates plus an optional one
to Win 8 went on without a problem.

Clark Morris  
>
>YMMV.
>
>Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-08T11:56:37+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<al1257FpiiU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net> <1rGdna5yTMPb7XXNnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <8prle8touii8kbfo50ajr1pslj3ss0g6bg@4ax.com> <kcf2a7$cp4$1@dont-email.me>`

```
Julian wrote:
> On 07/01/2013 16:55, SkippyPB wrote:
>> On Sat, 5 Jan 2013 13:26:25 -0500, "Charles Hottel"
…[84 more quoted lines elided]…
> by it also being the toush screen element?l*

A fair and perceptive observation.

Win8 CAN be used entirely without touch (I use it that way every day) but 
people who won't even use a trackpad on a notebook are probably not going to 
enjoy the transition. (and they don't HAVE to make it...)

I'm not on commission for MS and I really don't care whether people here use 
Win 8 or not. I responded to a valid question which was asked about touch 
screens  and replied with information based on my own experience. Obviously, 
my response is opinion and therefore arguable.

The point here, Julian, is that "people" (as the great majority of  the 
millions of Win 8 users) are NOT troubled by it; there are many of us who DO 
want a unified interface across all devices and Win 8 has allowed people to 
get this and still retain what they are used to while they absorb the newer 
tiled app interface.

Your point about previous moaning and whinging is well taken, but it is SOME 
people, not everyone.

(It just happens that CLC is a gathering place for that section of the 
overall user community :-)  It seems that COBOL experience narrows the minds 
of many people; I think Dijkstra made a tongue-in-cheek comment to that 
effect. Nevertheless, I have been coming here for over 20 years and I see no 
reason to stop now. I will when it stops being fun, but that hasn't happened 
yet...It's kind of amusing to me that I mention in passing a keyboard 
shortcut for Windows which I wasn't aware of and there are then three posts 
about how it has always been there; I never suggested it wasn't there, just 
that I didn't know about it. I always closed apps by clicking the close 
gadget. Sadly I have never been in the position of knowing everything about 
everything and every time I learn a Windows shortcut I forget a VSAM reply 
code...:-))

Personally, after finding Win 98 ME to be a horror show, that experience 
coloured my judgement when it came time to move from XP (which I found to be 
an extremely satisfactory OS and the FIRST one from MS that I was ever 
completely happy with, although Win 98 SE was also good)  and I was 
skeptical about Vista and never went there. I found Win 7 Ultimate to be 
good but I never moved any of my development machines to it, staying with XP 
in the hope that Win 8 would be excellent.

It is, and I am in the process of now moving development onto it. It is 
especially useful for 64 bit development and 32 bit compatibility and I want 
all PRIMA software to run in 32 or 64 bit architectures across XP, Win 7 and 
Win 8. (At the moment this goal is not achieved but we are working on it and 
it is promising.)

 Ironically, the C# development has no problem at all with it and can be 
built to target "any CPU", but the COBOL is a millstone round our necks 
which holds us back to 32 bit .DLLs. (No, I refuse on principle to invest 
outrageous amounts of money into a new COBOL compiler, when it is not going 
to be part of the future for us...). Although a 64 bit application can 
successfully load and execute 32 bit .DLLs, it will never be as good as if 
they were 64 bit...  so the best option for us is to rewrite the COBOL 
components. That has risks attached and also takes time and energy (not to 
mention, money...) but the nettle is being grasped and by the end of this 
year I expect COBOL to have been phased out completely.

A part of me is sorry to see the COBOL go; it served us well and was fun to 
write, but there is no point in getting emotional over computer software. C# 
is many times more powerful and is also fun to write...and it is free.

As far as interfaces go (to get back on topic) the secret is to use layers 
and objects, just as we do with data access.

I have been turning my attention to XAML lately and it will be interesting 
to see where that leads. I'd like to have a migration path from PowerCOBOL 
to .Net (I still have some packages that are written in PowerCOBOL and I'd 
really like to modernise them).

PRIMA should have a tool to generate XAML from PowerCOBOL projects during 
the coming year and this will have something that turns PowerCOBOL 
scriptlets into a single Class that will be wired to the XAML screen  by 
the normal code-behind links (but is a completely separate entity) for event 
and procedure processing using WPF (Windows Presentation Foundation). The 
end result is that the PowerCOBOL screen can be developed/maintained using a 
design surface just as in PowerCOBOL projects, and the scriptlets can be 
maintained as methods in a single COM Class, using COBOL or C# (or whatever 
New Technology language or scripting you want to use) or a combination of 
both, so that it is easy to make the transition away from dependence on 
PowerCOBOL.

The point is that Presentation Layers (user interfaces) need the SAME 
separation as Data Layers do. And that's true whether it is CICS on a 
mainframe or Windows screens on a desktop, or phone apps on a touch surface. 
Business Logic should be isolated to send and receive information through 
buffers in memory (it should never be dependent on a particular data access 
system or presentation system), and the mechanics of how those buffers are 
serviced is a separate problem.

Pete.
```

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> 
> I realize that I don't like the idea of touch screen for my desktop PC. Why? 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> resistant to change as I get older.

I hated touch screens when I first encountered them as a kid (though 
they generally weren't actually touch-sensitive, but position-sensing 
using an array of IR LEDs and photodetectors parallel to the screen), 
and I hate them now. Nothing to do with getting older in my case.

I don't like mice, either. (All of my machines have pointer controls 
integrated into the keyboard.) The keyboard is a tremendously 
efficient and high-bandwidth input device, once you learn to use it; 
anything that takes my fingers off the home row is an impediment to my 
use.

And I won't want a touchscreen anyway until I get fitted with 
transparent fingers.

The Microsoft vision described in that PC World article Pete linked to 
sounds dreadful, from my point of view. I've used tablets and I find 
the UX (user experience) and UIM (user interaction model) horrible, 
for me as a user. Perhaps they're wonderful for many, even most, other 
users; but they're by no means ideal for everyone.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-08T12:27:33+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<al13v7F1613U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net> <1rGdna5yTMPb7XXNnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <kcfj3v028as@news3.newsguy.com>`

```
Michael Wojcik wrote:
> Charles Hottel wrote:
>>
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> users; but they're by no means ideal for everyone.

Fair comment, Michael.

The important point is that people have an option.

I tried to make the point that the rising generation do not generally see 
computers in the same way that most of us do. How could they? Their 
experience is vastly different from someone who has worked with programming 
mainframes or even desktops.

Personally, I'd love to see the institution of the "touch and movement" 
interfaces described in the referenced article, but I also think that you 
can't force people to ANY kind of interface. At the end of the day, if an 
interface is really uncomfortable for some people, then those people just 
won't use it. If it is interfacing to functionality that they HAVE to have 
then they will be dragged kicking and screaming into it or they will find an 
alternative that doesn't even involve computers... :-)

I don't think it is "wrong" for people to complain about an interface if 
they personally don't like it. I DO think it is incumbent on software 
developers to provide alternatives (and that is why the separation of 
objects and layers that I keep on about is so important; it facilitates more 
than one kind of presentation service, for example. Windows 8 does not force 
you to use touch, but it does allow you to become accustomed to a tiled 
interface with apps, at your own speed.).

An example:

When I first started using Win 8 I used IE8 on the desktop because that is 
what I know. The fact that there is a Google tile on the Start screen didn't 
mean that much. But now, around 5 weeks into Win 8, I'm perfectly 
comfortable using the tiled Google (which opens IE9) and am finding my way 
round that. So, if I'm working on the desktop, rather than press the Windows 
key and go to the start screen, I just use the desktop IE icon. But if I am 
on the start screen and have been trying out some apps, rather than switch 
to the desktop to run IE (which I did at first) I just use the tile for 
Google and do my browse from there.

In another 25 years much of what we are discussing here will be moot and 
everyone will use "touch and movement" to get what they need from computers. 
Or they won't... :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Tue, 8 Jan 2013 04:23:47 -0800 (PST), Alistair Maclean
<alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Tuesday, 8 January 2013 05:38:40 UTC, Pete Dashwood wrote:

Unless your web-site has something I really care about, if Firefox
can't handle it, I don't bother using it.
```

```
My wife carries an epi-pen for citrus reaction and has a number of
other food intolerances.  When her resistance is down even a small
amount of gluten, cabbage family products, whey or a number of other
things can make her sick.  When her immune system is in good shape

member of the order of the dripping nose) and she has to be careful
about how long she is in a place with dogs or cats.  She has taken
something that seems to have reduced her vulnerability there.
Restaurants that serve relatively plain food many times do better for
us than the higher end ones. Not being able to eat foods you like is
no fun and my wife likes several of the ones she is allergic to.
Thankfully for both of us she is an imaginative and good cook.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-09T08:41:57-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kcjsdg02813@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<al13v7F1613U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net> <1rGdna5yTMPb7XXNnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <kcfj3v028as@news3.newsguy.com> <al13v7F1613U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> I tried to make the point that the rising generation do not generally see 
> computers in the same way that most of us do. How could they? Their 
> experience is vastly different from someone who has worked with programming 
> mainframes or even desktops.

A few points on that topic:

- Certainly there are tremendous differences in the end-user computing 
experience today, versus that of, say, twenty or thirty years ago. 
"User-visible" computing devices (as opposed to embedded ones that 
users often aren't even aware of) are ubiquitous among the middle 
class in the developed world. For many users, portable devices from 
PDAs and laptops through smartphones and tablets mean even 
general-purpose computers are almost always available.

- However, many of the most psychologically significant aspects of the 
end user experience haven't changed discernibly over that time. OSes 
and applications are still plagued by bugs and vulnerabilities. The 
user experience is full of random reinforcement - similar behaviors 
get different results. All of the problems Platt describes in _Why 
Software Sucks_ (a book that should be mandatory reading for anyone 
who develops user-facing software) still exist: programmers write 
software for themselves rather than for users, etc. Even firms that do 
usability research often make it secondary or do it piecemeal, 
focusing on a handful of techniques.

- The so-called "digital natives" - people who grew up with ubiquitous 
computing - are often assumed by pundits to be more skilled and 
knowledgeable users, but methodologically-sound studies have refuted 
those claims in a number of areas. That shouldn't be surprising. After 
a century of widespread automobile use, many car owners and drivers 
can't change a tire or check their oil, and in places that don't have 
self-service gas stations, often don't even know how to fuel their 
vehicles.

- It's a truism of the UI/UX field that "intuitive" means "what the 
user is already used to". So in that sense, each "generation" 
(obviously a term of convenience, since there's really no such thing) 
of users will have different expectations of computers, since they'll 
have had different experiences of them. But the assumptions made by 
the likes of _Wired_ writers about those users and their expectations 
do not hold up well under scrutiny.

Things - users, expectations, interfaces - change, yes. But we should 
be leery of trying to predict those changes.

> I don't think it is "wrong" for people to complain about an interface if 
> they personally don't like it. I DO think it is incumbent on software 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> interface with apps, at your own speed.).

Alternatives are also important for accessibility, of course. A user 
who suffers hand tremors probably won't want a touch interface (at 
least of the "start here and swipe there and then there" variety). And 
they're important for different user roles; a touch or gestural UI 
that might work for casual content browsing becomes terribly tiresome 
if you have to repeat an action for any length of time, and is an RSI 
disaster if you have to use it continuously for the entire work day.

Some of you may have seen the film _Minority Report_; in an early 
sequence Tom Cruise's character is using a gestural UI to search some 
video. The scene is popular with some of the more wild-eyed "UI of the 
future" types, but when I watch it all I can think is that he'll need 
a wrist brace by the end of the week.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-10T12:15:22-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kcmu5t0emn@news6.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<c52re81us9httlbe6vodjidmvijckjgbe4@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net> <1rGdna5yTMPb7XXNnZ2dnUVZ_hWdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <kcfj3v028as@news3.newsguy.com> <al13v7F1613U1@mid.individual.net> <kcjsdg02813@news3.newsguy.com> <c52re81us9httlbe6vodjidmvijckjgbe4@4ax.com>`

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> 
> The "gestural" UI may not be that far off.  Already Wii has a
> "gesture" based interface for its games.

Oh, gestural UIs are definitely here already. Besides the Wii (gesture 
recognition through handheld accelerometers), there's the Xbox's 
Kinect peripheral, which does visual gesture recognition; and people 
have implemented gestural UIs with smartphones and other devices.

Despite my disdain for the concept in general, I actually own a Texas 
Instruments eZ430-Chronos, which is a digital watch that incorporates 
numerous sensors and Bluetooth communications, plus the SDK for it. 
The eZ430 has accelerometers, and people have used it for gestural 
control of presentation software and the like. (I got one to play 
around, though I haven't done anything with it yet.)

> Flutter, a start-up in Palo Alto, California, is allowing anyone with
> a Mac or Windows computer and webcam to download an application that
> allows them to control music and video applications such as Spotify,
> iTunes, Windows Media Player, QuickTime, and VLC using gestures.

Yes. It's basically a simplified Kinect. The Kinect benefits from 
using multiple cameras.

Then there's the *original* Microsoft Surface (now "PixelSense") - not 
the tablet, but the coffee-table-computer-thing they came out with 
back in 2007. (They actually sold a few of those, mostly to hotel 
chains like Sheraton.) People generally described the old Surface as a 
"touchscreen coffee table", but it actually used cameras inside the 
table to recognize objects placed on and moved across its surface.

> And MIT in 2010 developed something akin to what was seen in Minority
> Report.  See
> http://web.mit.edu/newsoffice/2010/gesture-computing-0520.html

Yes. It's still a lousy idea for any sort of long-term or repetitive work.

There are also 2D mouse-based gestural interfaces. The Opera browser 
was one of the first pieces of commercial software to promote them - 
it introduced mouse gestures for common operations like "back" and 
"forward". Other browsers picked the idea up, but it never seemed to 
become very popular.

For a decades, people have been experimenting with eye-tracking as an 
input channel. (It's long been used in UI research to study user 
behavior, so trying to do more with it is an obvious direction.) These 
days you can find free software that tracks your glance using a webcam 
and tries to do something useful with it, like changing keyboard 
focus. (That would be disastrous for me, since like many touch-typists 
I often don't look at the window I'm typing in.)

Gestural interfaces work well for some applications - conducting an 
orchestra, directing taxiing airplanes, semaphore and sign language - 
but they have severe limitations that restrict their general 
applicability. They tend to have poor ergonomics, as I've already 
noted. They have a poor signal/noise ratio. Recognition systems need 
to distinguish between meaningful and accidental movements, which 
either means a lot of context processing by the system (and even then 
it's hard to get decent failure rates) or an excessive burden on 
users, who have to restrict and/or exaggerate their natural movements.

It's like anything else. A research team or a company comes out with 
something. They're all excited about it because they've been working 
on it and they're invested. Often there's a flurry of interest in the 
press, or among the pundits, or even in the mass market (as with the 
Wii). Then the thrill wears off and folks realize that no one mode of 
interaction is best suited for every use.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```
In article <akqfnaFffj1U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[snip]

>The mindset regarding computers and the use of them that most people here 
>hold, is NOT the mindset of the rising generation.

An interesting point, Mr Dashwood, and one which might be more interesting 
to take a bit further.

'Most people here' - I have taken no survey - are, quite possibly, 
programmers or folks with a 'programming background'.  As such their 
mindsets might include more of 'what can I make the computer do for 
others' than it would of 'what can I make the computer do for me'.

One of the points that might need to be re-visited, regularly, is 'How is 
the foundation of the past to be retained?'

Those who reply 'it doesn't' have, most likely, never dealt with 
purchasing property.  The legal right to convey title to a portion of land 
is not, in most places, taken lightly.  This might be considered alongside 
of one of the oldest disciplines of mathematics, earth-measure... ummmm, 
geometry.

DD
```

```
I don't know if anyone is suggesting that touch screens will take over the 
world; I'm certainly not.

However, I do believe touch SURFACES (where the surface could be anything 
handy, including your arms and legs), and the interaction of MOVEMENT with a 
computer, as illustrated in the link I already posted, will "take over the 
world", inasmuch as future interactions with computers are likely to be 
through an ENVIRONMENT rather than directly with a device.

However, it will be some time yet and some of us here are unlikely to see 
it.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ OT: In defence of science fiction WAS: Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-13T10:53:00+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ale49tFu14pU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <8b432fab-0a45-49a9-9951-81eaf46753c1@googlegroups.com> <albd2gFbaduU1@mid.individual.net> <qv23f8t0jv1dmmm8svcja0b5npb2mtpmjl@4ax.com> <aldhjbFpknaU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <qv23f8t0jv1dmmm8svcja0b5npb2mtpmjl@4ax.com>,
> SkippyPB <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> writes:
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
> Imagination always outpaces reality by miles.

Not "always"...(and it depends what you mean by "miles")

Mobile phones (exactly to the saturation we have right now), waste disposal 
devices,  smart kitchen devices, and home entertainment centres (large 
screen TV with home theatre), were all predicted by Ray Bradbury in his 
1950s short story "The Murderer" and all came to pass within 40 years... 
(some sooner). Is that "miles"?

Arthur C. Clarke envisioned communication satellites in geostationary orbits 
in the early 1950s and the Russians launched Sputnik 1 in 1957... Telstar 
followed in the early 60s... definitely not "miles" behind.

Men walked on the Moon within 7 years of the US President declaring that an 
American must.

It is an arguable proposition that reality lags way behind imagination, but 
one thing that is NOT arguable is that, WITHOUT the imagination we would not 
be enjoying the comforts we take for granted through technology.

If there is no aspiration, there will be no achievement.

It is the imagination that lets us postulate a better way.

Sometimes we bite off more than we can chew (like nuclear technology which 
had such huge promise and also such huge risk), but in learning to deal with 
that we grow as a race. Despite gloomy forecasts of annihilation through 
nuclear disaster before the turn of the last century, we are all still here. 
Both Russia and America looked into that particular abyss and decided it was 
not inviting. As a result, kids today are not growing up under the threat of 
global extinction by Mutually Assured Destruction, as we had to do. There is 
still a risk that a lunatic or desperate nation could acquire nuclear 
weapons and take everybody down, but it is a far smaller risk than it was 50 
years ago. We face other kinds of risk now and those too are being 
addressed.

There is nothing to fear from imagination.


> Just pick up a copy
> of Popular Science from the 50's.  Or listen to old radio shows like
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> bill

It would be a hard man who whould expect EVERY single thing predicted by 
science fiction to become a reality within a short space of time.

As development proceeds it becomes apparent that a particular line of 
thought is not viable or is superseded by a different approach (bit like 
developing computer systems, really... :-)).

If you read Kurzweil's "The Singularity is Near" (and it is a book well 
worth reading...or at least browsing through) he shows that the RATE of
technological advancement is increasing. Something that might have taken 100 
years to develop 200 years ago, takes 20 years now. By the end of this 
century it will take 5 years.

Alvin Toffler identified the effect that rapid change has on human beings in 
his landmark book, "Future Shock", in 1970. But since that was written we 
have produced a couple of generations of kids who are much more resistant to 
that syndrome than we were in the 70s.

It is the nature of humanity to create situations and environments, then 
adapt to them.

"Science fiction" is an essential part of exploring the possibillities 
through imagination. The fact that every single thing predicted doesn't come 
to pass or that sometimes it takes half a century to materialize, doesn't 
make the process any less valuable.

Pete.
```

```
as a navigational aid for sailors, permitting them to determine
longitude ..."  "It's 37 inhabitants signaled the Earth in Morse code
by jumping up and down on the outside of the satellite."

Extracts from 'History of Rocketry and Space Travel' by Werner Von
Braun and Frederick I Ordway IIII.
```

```
pauses after each word.

I don't think that developing speech recognition is mutually exclusive to 
developing an interactive environment, in fact, I see them as complementary.
```

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Saturday, January 12, 2013 11:57:28 PM UTC, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> A time may come when the Internet is extended to chips inside our
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> sundry.

Well, for me it will be academic as I don't expect to be here in 2047, but I 
imagine that if this does come to pass (and there would have to be some HUGE 
shifts in the general population's relationship with technology), there 
would be possibilities to select and filter the connections you initiated 
and accepted.

It is also worth noticing that by that time we will all have artificially 
boosted intelligence and memory, so we would probably be better able to cope 
with it than we can with our current evolved ape brains...

I am working at the moment on a historical novel and the other day I was 
thinking about when would have been the best time to have been born. I 
decided that the mid to latter part of the 20th century was a pretty good 
time (once we got over the nuclear war nonsense). We had proper plumbing and 
mod cons, the food was pretty good and for most of us freedom to live our 
lives and pursue happiness was pretty much a given. There were exciting new 
breakthroughs in science and technology, and the sum total of Human 
knowledge surged for the first time to a level where no single person could 
encompass it. (It became necessary to develop specializations, rather than 
"university"...)

Currently, I am  in what I call the "crepuscular" stage of what has been a 
very interesting and stimulating life. It is a good time to reflect and 
postulate. The adventuring is largely over, but I really don't miss it and 
there are other gentler things that give me equal pleasure.

I am content. :-)

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <8b432fab-0a45-49a9-9951-81eaf46753c1@googlegroups.com> <albd2gFbaduU1@mid.individual.net> <qv23f8t0jv1dmmm8svcja0b5npb2mtpmjl@4ax.com> <ale207Ftgq2U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Sun, 13 Jan 2013 10:13:41 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>SkippyPB wrote:
>> On Sat, 12 Jan 2013 10:04:14 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
>developing an interactive environment, in fact, I see them as complementary.



I think it's less a "bypassed" technology, but one that's just now
becoming actually useful.  Omitting things that don't work well makes
considerable sense.  OTOH, current versions work remarkably well (far
from perfect, but certainly from a historical perspective).  IIRC,
you're not a smartphone guy, but current versions of iPhones and
Android based phones have fairly decent speech recognition.

Despite decade of speech input being "just around the corner", it
hasn't been until the last few years that it's actually been useful.
When speech input systems started making their way into voice response
systems about a decade ago, it was enough to make you want put your
phone through the wall.  These days they're actually reasonably
accurate.  And what you can get on your PC or smartphone is
considerably better.  Still far from perfect, though, particularly if
you use somewhat unusual word patterns.  My Android just rendere the
opening of the Gettysburg address as "4 just brought forth on this
continent a new nation conceived in Liberty and dedicated to the
proposition that all men are created equal".  Close but not quite, but
if I replaced the "four score and seven" with "eighty-seven", it got
it right "87 years ago our forefathers brought forth upon this
continent a new nation conceived in Liberty and dedicated to the
proposition that all men are created equal").  Also note the
interpretation of "liberty" as a proper noun.

Speech's fundamental problem is that it's not a very good interface
device for controlling anything, but it clearly has an application
where only limited interaction is required, or where constraints make
other interfaces impractical.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: What is your opinion regardinf touch screens?

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-14T08:16:09-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kd111o026g2@news1.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<qv23f8t0jv1dmmm8svcja0b5npb2mtpmjl@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<poCdnbIpdJe6OHrNnZ2dnUVZ_rGdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <8b432fab-0a45-49a9-9951-81eaf46753c1@googlegroups.com> <albd2gFbaduU1@mid.individual.net> <qv23f8t0jv1dmmm8svcja0b5npb2mtpmjl@4ax.com>`

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> 
> While what Pete is suggesting may be true and come to pass, I'd rather
> the engineers spend their energies perfecting speech recognition both
> in coming and out going.

There is a *huge* amount of research being done in the various natural 
language processing (NLP) fields, including speech recognition (SR) - 
primary and applied, academic and commercial. There are dozens of 
major conferences and journals, and it's one of the larger and growing 
areas not just for CS students and new grads but for many people 
working in cognate fields; there are a lot of people in the humanities 
doing NLP research, thanks to interest in (and funding for) "big data" 
projects.

People have been trying to "perfect" SR for decades, and they've 
basically gotten to the point where it's usable for some purposes, by 
some people, some of the time. It's not at all clear that pouring more 
resources into it would make it get better any faster.

And SR is a terrible input mechanism for many problems. Try reciting 
source code or a spreadsheet, for example. There's a reason we use 
symbolic notation and high-bandwidth, high-signal input devices 
(keyboards, since no one's come up with anything else even close) for 
a lot of applications.

That said, it is an interesting field, and it's very easy to 
participate if you're so inclined. You can pick up a second-hand copy 
of Jurafsky & Martin's /Speech and Language Processing/ relatively 
cheaply, you almost certainly have adequate hardware for many 
projects, and you can use free software infrastructure. (I use a 
combination of plain ol' Java and IBM/Apache UIMA - both free - for my 
NLP research. For SR, you could add the free CMU Sphinx software.)
```

<a id="t-f86d9f95aa"></a>
## Thread: [OT] L'esprit d'escalier regardinf Interface Changes — 4 msgs

### [OT] L'esprit d'escalier regardinf Interface Changes

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2013-01-06T23:56:18+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kcd2v1$e8i$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```

As regards the recent back-and-forth about the new Microsft interface and 
the related loss of ease/productivity incurred during a period of 
familiarisation:

Back in the Oldene Dayse, before attention-enslaving telephones or 
personal computers or mainframe computers or moving-picture shows... a 
fair amount of heavy industry in the United States of America relied on 
machinery powered by water.

Essentially the 'eternal' motion of a river would used to rotate a large, 
heavy shaft; the rest of the machinery would use this shaft's energy by 
the means of belts and eccentric cams and the like.  Researching the 
intricacies of these methods might prove engaging.

In the late 1880s Thomas Edison, George Westinghouse and a few others 
began to make large-scale production of electricity available.  This 
allowed factories to free themselves from the dictates of a central 
shaft's motion powering individual machines.

It took a few decades for this freedom to be disseminated into factory 
design.  One reason might be that everyone who would trust or be trusted 
with the task of designing a factory had been trained on water-powered 
systems.

DD
```

#### ↳ Re: [OT] L'esprit d'escalier regardinf Interface Changes

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-06T20:35:58-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<pmcke8tnq3t6l26pbcrnk1asfumfcj4g24@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<kcd2v1$e8i$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
On Sun, 6 Jan 2013 23:56:18 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:

>
>As regards the recent back-and-forth about the new Microsft interface and 
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>systems.


"Central shaft" factory power systems were also run via steam power
and (centralized) electric motors.  As electric motors became cheaper,
and preexisting (externally driven) machinery needed to be replaced,
that became more decentralized (so you went through a phase were
having several machines run off a single electric motor was common),
until we got to the electric motor per-machine stage.

Water power was attractive for its low cost, assuming you had the
proper site for your factory.  Water power was also subject to a
number of external factors limiting its reliability, although, of
course, early steam power was not particularly reliable either.
```

## Thread: Getting started with Cobol 2002 + — 65 msgs

### Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

- **From:** Patrick <halfmaddad@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-11T05:15:32-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com>`

```
Hello Everyone

This is my first post here.

I have been learning Ada for about 14 months now(after the kids go to bed). It seems that I have learned a bit of Fortran, Pascal and Cobol as a result.

I don't really like the older Cobol code that seems to require certain things in certain columns but the newer Cobol 2002 syntax looks really nice. The only things is a can't really find enough resources for it.

I have open Cobol working fine but are there any other compilers I should check out?

Are there any good Cobol 2002+ tutorials out there?

I found "COBOL for the 21st Century, 11th Edition", are there any other modern Cobol books you recommend?

Is there a repository of shared Cobol code somewhere?

Thanks for reading-Patrick
```

```
Patrick wrote:
> Hi HeyBub
>
> Thanks for your answer. Cobol 2002+ is OO too no? Are there good
> books and tutorials discussing this?

You can see an example of standard COBOL '85 being converted to OO COBOL in 
order to provide a free tool  (COBOL structure analyser, it helps other 
language programmers get offsets and lengths into COBOL defined data 
buffers...) which several hundred people have found useful, at:

http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21

This site is currently being completely overhauled and I am hoping to 
relaunch it by the end of February. At that time there will be a new 
tutorial on OO COBOL 101 aimed at helping traditional COBOL people get their 
heads around Objects and Layers...

OO COBOL is excellent for writing components and for making the transition 
between Standard COBOL and the world of OO languages. However, it is not a 
good long term solution for OO development because other OO languages are 
better and cheaper. Sadly, Open COBOL does not support OO and the last time 
I spoke with Roger there were no plans to do so. If it did, I would 
certainly be using it.

Currently the boundaries between the COBOL site above and the PRIMA 
computing site are a bit blurred, so that is being addressed as well.

Both sites will share a common landing page with the divisions between what 
is free and what is not clearly shown. It is a major redevelopment and I am 
spending most of my time on it currently.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-15T08:37:40-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kd3m3m02iqc@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com> <kcpj4j$ecg$1@reader1.panix.com> <cc0446ae-2fe1-4f71-a792-29b8cb66e6e2@googlegroups.com> <09SdnT377JTcmmnNnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com>`

```
Patrick wrote:
> 
> Cobol 2002+ is OO too no? Are there good books and tutorials discussing this?


Rui Bivar de Oliveira's book _The Power of COBOL_ (ISBN 0-620-34652-3) 
has some chapters on OO COBOL, using ISO (standard) syntax.[1]

You might be able to find a copy of Ray Obin's _Object Orientation: An 
Introduction for COBOL Programmers_, published by Micro Focus (ISBN 
1-56928-001-0). It's based on the first Micro Focus OO COBOL, from 
long before there was a standard,[2] so the syntax it describes is not 
COBOL 2002. But it's short and readable and a good conceptual 
introduction to OO COBOL, though programmers familiar with OO concepts 
will know a lot of the material already.

There are some examples of ISO OO COBOL programs on the web - if you 
use a sufficiently specific search ('"configuration section" 
repository "class-id"', for example) you can find some.


However: I don't believe OpenCOBOL supports OO COBOL of any sort, so 
you won't be able to try out OO COBOL with it. I don't know that there 
are any free compilers that do have OO COBOL support. (The free 
edition of Micro Focus Enterprise Developer only does syntax checking 
- it doesn't produce executables - and you can only get the Academic 
edition through a partner institution. I don't know what's currently 
available from Fujitsu.)



[1] There are several versions of OO COBOL. There's the standard 
syntax and semantics from ISO 1989-2002, the current published 
international COBOL standard. It's a bit verbose (in keeping with 
COBOL in general). There were older, pre-standard OO extensions to 
COBOL from some of the COBOL vendors, including Micro Focus. Then, 
after the 2002 standard, Micro Focus introduced alternative, 
simplified OO syntax for the managed environments (.NET and JVM); 
other vendors may have created something similar. The 2002 version of 
OO COBOL is standard; the simplified syntax is more convenient.

[2] Obin's book was published in 1993, so it predates the ISO standard 
by nearly a decade. He wrote it not long after the X3J5.1 committee 
produced its technical report, the first step toward the eventual 
standard.
```
