###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 7)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-16T14:24:52+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<almdr5Fqo2lU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com> <kcpj4j$ecg$1@reader1.panix.com> <cc0446ae-2fe1-4f71-a792-29b8cb66e6e2@googlegroups.com> <09SdnT377JTcmmnNnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com> <kd3m3m02iqc@news3.newsguy.com> <4a35874c-f7c1-4c41-b800-344052896ddc@googlegroups.com>`

```
Patrick wrote:
> Thanks Pete and Michael
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> and easy to read.

I think you have to place the "dire warnings" in context.

I used COBOL successfully for over 30 years. It was very good and mostly did 
what was required.

But the world moved on.

The way that most people and corporations use computers today is NOT the way 
we did in the 1960s.

Networked operations need small pieces of code that  do clearly defined 
functions and require nothing more (they are "self-contained"). Objects meet 
this reqirement perfectly. Objects derived from Classes are encapsulated and 
they have their own properties (data) and methods (behaviours) which enable 
them to deliver functionality directly and wherever it is needed. They can 
respond to events happening in the real world and can even decide to provide 
more clones of themselves to handle unusual loads. It is a different 
paradigm and different concept from processing linearly and controlling 
everything from within a program.

Object Orientation is too important to be ignored and the world has not 
ignored it.

By the time COBOL was enhanced to support OO, the world was already moving 
to other languages that were DESIGNED to support OO. There is very little 
that you can't do with a modern COBOL compiler that supports OO, but other 
languages do it "better". (how and why they are better is too much to go 
into here, but if you talk to people who are familiar with both COBOL and a 
modern language like C#, Java, even VB.Net, they will tell you about 
conciseness of code, better tools than have typically been available for 
COBOL, and infinitely better support and help with learning.) You CAN do 
stuff with COBOL just like you CAN make fire by rubbing sticks together, but 
there are easier, faster, much more efficient ways to achieve a result.

Since moving from COBOL to C#, my personal productivity has more than 
doubled (and I was no slouch in COBOL...:-))  The cost of maintaining code 
has more than halved (but some of that is due to better design techniques 
and moving to largely component based development.)

COBOL is expensive; other languages are free.

The world hasn't gone off COBOL because everybody hates it; they have done 
so because it is much less relevant in today's world than was once the case.

http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21/COBOLRelevance.aspx     (Has taken just 
over 1000 hits)

http://java.dzone.com/articles/cretaceous-cobol-can-spawn   (Has taken 
around 11,500 hits)

You should also be aware that nobody has fully implemented the COBOL 2002 
specification yet.

(Having said that, it is an excellent spec.; probably too late to catch the 
last train leaving COBOL Town...)

>I am going to drop C++ and use Cobol, Ada and Lua.
> A weird but powerful trio

Good luck with that :-)

Maybe you could supplement your COBOL study with Sanskrit and Latin....? :-)


>
> Thanks for the tips, history and links

Seriously... wish you well with your development.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 8)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-16T17:42:55-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kd7aej02tn1@news4.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<almdr5Fqo2lU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com> <kcpj4j$ecg$1@reader1.panix.com> <cc0446ae-2fe1-4f71-a792-29b8cb66e6e2@googlegroups.com> <09SdnT377JTcmmnNnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com> <kd3m3m02iqc@news3.newsguy.com> <4a35874c-f7c1-4c41-b800-344052896ddc@googlegroups.com> <almdr5Fqo2lU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> By the time COBOL was enhanced to support OO, the world was already moving 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> COBOL, and infinitely better support and help with learning.)

"Better" is debatable.

I'm pretty proficient in COBOL, including various versions of 
procedural COBOL and OO COBOL. But my background is in CS and 
programming languages; before I learned COBOL I was proficient in 
BASIC and Pascal, and since then I've done serious work in C, C++, 
Fortran, LISP, Scheme, ML, OCaml and F#, x86 assembly, VAX assembly, 
Java, C#, and more scripting languages than I can count, with a 
smattering of others. I've studied a good bit of programming language 
design, written lexers and parsers and compilers, created my own 
domain-specific languages. I've followed language standardization 
processes and studied standards and their peripheral documents 
(rationales and the like); at one time I nearly had the C90 standard 
memorized. (The majority of my professional programming has been in 
C.) I've presented at academic conferences on how programming language 
design affects software development. So I have some experience in this 
area.

Here's the thing: there's good reason to believe that for most 
programming, the largest economic advantage comes from *readable* 
code. For most organizations, maintenance is the largest part of 
software-development costs (some studies estimate as high as 80%); and 
in most of those, a large part of maintenance is understanding 
existing code.

Consequently, "concise code" is a false idol. *Expressive* programming 
languages are good, because they let the source code correspond more 
closely to the algorithm, and make it easier to convey the program's 
purpose to the reader. But many programming language designs favor 
concision over clarity. OCaml (and other languages in the ML family) 
is a marvelous language in many ways, but it uses idiosyncratic 
punctuation for so many operations that it becomes nearly unreadable 
if you don't use it frequently. The champion of concise source, among 
languages that are used for serious purposes, is APL - infamous for 
its "write-only" nature.

C and C++ aren't as bad as OCaml (or anywhere near as bad as APL - but 
then nothing is) in this regard, but they make writing awkward, 
unclear code easy, and they've accumulated programming cultures that 
encourage it. I've worked with a lot of C and C++ developers, and read 
and changed a lot of other people's C and C++ source (closed and 
open), and the vast majority of it is terrible, from a readability 
point of view. The Java-family languages are better, but there's still 
a lot of dreadful source there.

COBOL has its own problems with readability (despite being designed in 
large part just for that - but under constraints that no longer 
apply). Procedural COBOL encourages huge monolithic programs, and its 
division-based structure creates huge reference problems: definitions 
far from point of use, flattened hierarchy, lousy encapsulation and 
abstraction. Even OO COBOL with simplified syntax (like MF's managed 
COBOL dialect) still suffers from artifacts of traditional COBOL, such 
as level numbers (pointless in OO COBOL). But COBOL at least 
acknowledges readability as a goal.

(There have been any number of attempts to address the readability 
problem over the years, from COBOL's documentary features like the 
identification division and the note statement, to in-code 
documentation systems like Javadoc, to coding standards like "picture 
on a page", to Knuth's "literate programming". In-code documentation 
is moderately popular; the others are mostly confined to niches. 
Literate programming, ironically, may have failed in part because the 
syntax Knuth developed for it is awful - a bunch of arbitrary 
sequences of punctuation.)

Programming languages certainly affect software development, and some 
are certainly better than others, particularly in specific domains. 
But the languages that can be called "worse" in an unqualified sense 
are *clearly* worse - they lack basic features or impose unnecessary 
limitations. Those languages aside, it's very hard to justify the 
claim that one programming language is better than another in any 
absolute sense.

I take your points about tooling and resources - but there again 
things are mostly subjective. Much of my early training used IDEs, but 
I've never cared for them, except sometimes when experimenting, for 
example; so as far as I'm concerned, the tools available for all the 
major languages are pretty much equivalent. And while there are a lot 
more examples and discussions and whatnot for C, say, online than 
there are for COBOL, the quality tends to be abysmal; if you're not 
reading material posted by someone who's intimately familiar with the 
C standard, it's likely wrong, or at least non-optimal.

I don't love COBOL; even the streamlined managed dialects carry a lot 
of old cruft. But I can find plenty of faults in any language I've 
used, even old favorites like Scheme or OCaml. C# is pretty nice, but 
interest in non-Windows implementations seems to be declining, and the 
political climate at Microsoft has swung against .NET and managed 
code. Contrary to some rumor-mongers I don't think .NET is going away 
- if COBOL has taught us anything, it's that once a language reaches 
decent penetration it'll be around for a long time - but I wouldn't 
want to be a C# specialist right now; I have a feeling that would 
involve a lot of arguing for my pet language in new projects. Java is 
very widely used, but its generics mechanism isn't very well designed, 
the framework's looking a bit dated, and Oracle's introducing security 
holes faster than they fix them. And so on.

My advice would be to learn a lot of languages, languages from 
different families and with different features and approaches. If 
you're comfortable with CS, I'd suggest studying language design a 
bit, maybe by reading something like the Lambda the Ultimate blog once 
in a while.
```

Given the above information, Patrick, COBOL may be ideal for what you have

Good programmers can write maintainable code in ANY language they are 
proficient in; poor programmers will always make a pig's ear of it in any 
language.

specific area and for what you want, standard COBOL is probably very 
adequate. As you can get it for free through Open COBOL, I can't see any 
reason why you wouldn't use it.

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Patrick wrote:
>>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>> from it of a year or longer.

Reading your own code is often a challenge for professional developers 
too, to be perfectly frank. (This has often come up in studies of 
software maintenance costs.) Even among experienced teams you'll hear 
a lot of "why did I do that?". The problem, of course, is not so much 
reading as it is understanding both the programmer's intention and the 
actual operation of the code.

> But ANY language (once you learn it) becomes "readable" and I have C# code I 
> wrote years ago (while I was still struggling to learn the basics ) that I 
> wouldn't hesitate to pick up and amend if the need arose.

Well, not *any* language - most of the "esoteric" languages (the 
deliberately difficult ones) are pretty unreadable regardless of how 
much time you spend with them. I doubt there's a human being alive who 
finds it easy to read Unlambda programs, and Malbolge is widely 
considered to be unwritable, much less readable. Among languages 
people actually use for real work, "figure out what this code does" is 
a popular hobby for APL programmers, and then of course there's the 
IOCCC (International Obfuscated C Coding Contest).

But *well-written* code in most languages (APL may always be an 
exception) should be easy to read years later. The key is to remember 
you're writing for two audiences: machine and human, and the machine 
doesn't care about clarity. So write primarily for the human. Put in a 
lot of meaningful comments: in this section I'm going to do x y z 
because a b c. Use whitespace so the "page" isn't cluttered. Use 
meaningful variable and procedure names.

By the way - C# is much closer to Java than it is to C and C++, and 
consequently makes less use of strange punctuation. You might find it 
more readable. (Other more-readable languages in the C family are Java 
itself and "Javascript", which is really a language named ECMAScript 
and has nothing to do with Java.)

> Good programmers can write maintainable code in ANY language they are 
> proficient in; poor programmers will always make a pig's ear of it in any 
> language.

Very true, aside from the especially unreadable languages. Programmer 
practices are by far the most important factor in readability.

It does help to use tools that you're comfortable with, and that give 
you the confidence to change things. Refactoring (changing working 
code to improve design and organization) is very important for keeping 
code maintainable, and you want to be free to do that without worrying 
that you'll break things. That means having a change-management system 
like Subversion or git (or any of the many other choices) so you can 
always back out changes; having an editor and build system that's 
convenient to use (IDE or separate components as you prefer); having a 
good debugger; having some tests so you can validate your changes.

> The real point for me is whether it is important any more, and I am becoming 
> persuaded that, with the advent of much smarter development tools and 
> systems, it probably isn't.

Often true, though in specific domains sometimes you want a language 
that's particularly suited for that type of work (or only a small set 
of languages are even available).
```

Michael Wojcik wrote:

> I'm pretty proficient in COBOL, including various versions of

Your credentials are not in question, Michael, but you should state that you 
are employed by a company with a very  heavily vested interest in COBOL.

> Here's the thing: there's good reason to believe that for most
> programming, the largest economic advantage comes from *readable*
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> existing code.

I agree that maintenance has traditionally been the largest single cost on 
companies developing and maintaining their own software. It is certainly 
true for sites where COBOL is used.

"Readable" code is a very fuzzy area. It has to do with the experience, 
training, interest, and dedication of the reader.
 One person may instantly read and grasp a code snippet, while another may 
struggle with it for some time. It is subjective.

Those of us who grew up with COBOL had it instilled in us that a major 
advantage was the possibility to write "English like" syntax that would be 
more "readable". We never questioned it because it seemed obvious. In fact, 
though, there were some down sides to this. Code could easily become verbose 
and require much more writing than needed to be the case. That takes longer 
to create and longer to scan through it when it has to be amended.

However, the emphasis  (at least for some people, and I have been one of 
them for a number of years now...) is changing. Where component based 
systems are used, there is FAR LESS emphasis on code because code is seldom 
maintained. FUNCTIONALITY is what is important.

If you are building with Lego you just replace a faulty or damaged block; 
you don't try and remould the plastic.

In terms of CS, the nature of code is important, but in terms of running a 
business, it isn't. It is functionality that is important.

I don't care whether code is readable or not if it does what I want it to.

If it doesn't do what I want it to I'll get another component that does... 
Classes and objects derived from them encapsulate FUNCTIONALITY. Some of it 
you may write yourself, some of it you may get from people with years of 
experience you could never hope to acquire. I don't have time (or 
inclination) to be expert in everything; it is enough to be clear about what 
I want to achieve and build or buy components that I can use to get there.

I have a number of components which I don't have source code for. They run 
every day and do precisely what they are specified to do. I trust them.

While the above might sound shocking to a traditional programmer, all of us 
are using components every day which we don't even have source code for, and 
therefore couldn't "maintain" even if we wanted to. The Windows Operating 
System I'm writing this under is composed of several thousand components. I 
trust them to do what I need and mostly, they do.

>
> Consequently, "concise code" is a false idol. *Expressive* programming
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> its "write-only" nature.

Yes, I came across some people in Madrid who were APL experts. What they 
achieved was staggering and the time they achieved it in was unthinkable. I 
saw an entire COBOL suite of 4 programs Say, a month's develpment effort by 
the time you include debugging and testing) replaced by APL code written 
from scratch in less than an hour.

However, it might has well have been Ancient Egyptian when you looked at 
it... But this comes back to my point above; "readability" depends on the 
reader. If you were prepared to finance and manage a team of APL experts, it 
could be a very viable way of running your business. (I wouldn't mind 
betting there are sites out there that do this...) Again, code matters to 
computer scientists. Computer scientists don't run your business.


>
> C and C++ aren't as bad as OCaml (or anywhere near as bad as APL - but
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> a lot of dreadful source there.

There's a lot of dreadful code in COBOL too. I have looked at and dealt with 
millions of lines of it, across decades, and across continents. COBOL is far 
from immune from "bad readability", despite the Holy Mantra to the 
contrary...
>
> COBOL has its own problems with readability (despite being designed in
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> acknowledges readability as a goal.

Agreed.

I think we disagree on how important that hard-to-define goal of 
"readibility" actually is.

>
> (There have been any number of attempts to address the readability
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> are certainly better than others, particularly in specific domains.

And that is also arguable. Everyone has their "favourite" and "least liked" 
programming languages. It is easy to lose sight of WHY we use ANY 
programming language: to provide functionality that makes our commercial 
life easier.

> But the languages that can be called "worse" in an unqualified sense
> are *clearly* worse - they lack basic features or impose unnecessary
> limitations. Those languages aside, it's very hard to justify the
> claim that one programming language is better than another in any
> absolute sense.

Agreed.

>
> I take your points about tooling and resources - but there again
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> C standard, it's likely wrong, or at least non-optimal.

But if it doesn't work, you can easily find an alternative...

>
> I don't love COBOL; even the streamlined managed dialects carry a lot
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> code.

Perhaps you hear different rumours from me. :-) I'm sure you don't act on 
hearsay any more than I do...



>Contrary to some rumor-mongers I don't think .NET is going away
> - if COBOL has taught us anything, it's that once a language reaches
> decent penetration it'll be around for a long time - but I wouldn't
> want to be a C# specialist right now; I have a feeling that would
> involve a lot of arguing for my pet language in new projects.

The politics of C# are way beyond the scope of this discussion. I think as 
long as Anders remains at MS the language will be used. Frankly, I'm not too 
bothered whether it is or not. (although, as a programmer, I have thoroughly 
enjoyed learning and using C#).  There is a rise in interest in XAML and 
Javascript and this is being driven by a whole new approach to how we use 
and develop applications.

The old days of "language wars" are going to become a thing of the past; 
you'll use tools that will generate code for you and do so in any language 
that is appropriate. You'll manipulate functions graphically and connect 
them up and  code will be automatically generated and regenerated in seconds 
whenever you decide to try your creation.

Legacy code, once it is refactored into objects, can play on this storyboard 
no matter what language it is written in.

> Java is
> very widely used, but its generics mechanism isn't very well designed,
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> in a while.

That is very sound advice.

It is also important to study business processes and iterative, interactive, 
development processes (Agile style) too. I believe this is important because 
it ensures fusion between the people writing the system and the people who 
will use it. That, again, comes back to understanding of the needed 
functionality, and the fact that those needs can change dynamically and 
fluidly, but can still be responded to and met. If you are driven by code 
development, constant change can be a dream wrecker, if you are driven by 
functionality and you are developing using components, (Classes/objects) it 
becomes an interesting and satisying exercise.

Code, and the readability of it, will become the least of our worries.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-17T11:12:00-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kd99se114cl@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<alp173Fe4sgU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com> <kcpj4j$ecg$1@reader1.panix.com> <cc0446ae-2fe1-4f71-a792-29b8cb66e6e2@googlegroups.com> <09SdnT377JTcmmnNnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com> <kd3m3m02iqc@news3.newsguy.com> <4a35874c-f7c1-4c41-b800-344052896ddc@googlegroups.com> <almdr5Fqo2lU1@mid.individual.net> <kd7aej02tn1@news4.newsguy.com> <alp173Fe4sgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>> Pete Dashwood wrote:

> Your credentials are not in question, Michael, but you should state that you 
> are employed by a company with a very  heavily vested interest in COBOL.

Well, it *is* in my sig. And I assume that everyone knows who I am 
anyway, as my fame has spread far and wide.

But fair point. Yes, COBOL underwrites my luxurious lifestyle (well, 
mostly it's the mainframe-emulation stuff, but COBOL is a big part of 
that). In this post I was really wearing my academic hat,[1] though, 
because code readability is one of my research areas. And I described 
my background because I was responding to your point about "people who 
are familiar with both COBOL and a modern language".

> I agree that maintenance has traditionally been the largest single cost on 
> companies developing and maintaining their own software. It is certainly 
> true for sites where COBOL is used.

Yes - the vast majority of the COBOL I've seen has been fairly awful 
to read, whatever the merits and faults of the language itself.

> "Readable" code is a very fuzzy area. It has to do with the experience, 
> training, interest, and dedication of the reader.
>  One person may instantly read and grasp a code snippet, while another may 
> struggle with it for some time. It is subjective.

Of course. I really wanted to make the point about not making "concise 
code" a virtue. Concision, when it expresses the intent and function 
of the code, is good; but it can easily be taken too far, and many 
languages (C, Perl, F#) encourage doing so, despite their other fine 
qualities. Excess verbosity is at least as bad, of course.

> Those of us who grew up with COBOL had it instilled in us that a major 
> advantage was the possibility to write "English like" syntax that would be 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> to create and longer to scan through it when it has to be amended.

Agreed. And "readable", besides being subjective, needs to be taken in 
context, of course. One of COBOL's syntactic advantages, in some 
applications, is in compliance: it's relatively straightforward to 
match the source to rules for calculating various values (for example 
in tax calculations). That's different from reading the code to figure 
out where to best insert a new feature, though.

This is one area where traditional COBOL's limited modularity really 
hurts it, because it's difficult to create the hierarchy of 
abstractions that you have in a well-written OO program, or even a 
well-written procedural or functional one. Traditional COBOL programs 
tend to be long blocks of monolithic code or a jumble of paragraphs 
with perform-thru and go to statements. COBOL 85 did improve on that 
with scope delimiters, in-line performs, and encouraging the use of 
simple performs for subroutines.

> However, the emphasis  (at least for some people, and I have been one of 
> them for a number of years now...) is changing. Where component based 
> systems are used, there is FAR LESS emphasis on code because code is seldom 
> maintained. FUNCTIONALITY is what is important.

Simple components may seldomly be maintained, but for most software I 
believe this simply isn't true. Commercial software is constantly 
being revised to maintain demand, and the shorter release cycles that 
have become normal in recent years have increased the rate of change. 
Embedded software, which until recently was usually impossible to 
update and so saw less maintenance than software for general-purpose 
systems, is now often stored in flash memory, so it's subject to the 
same kind of update pressures. Back-office systems have to be altered 
for new regulatory requirements and to add features, such as 
integration with other systems, driven by economic pressures. 
Web-based applications are constantly receiving cosmetic updates to 
try to retain and increase their user base.

Organizations like the ACM and IEEE publish a lot of studies on 
software development, and I haven't seen anyone offering hard data to 
show that the rate of software maintenance has dropped. The last time 
I read through some of these studies was a few years back, when I was 
preparing a presentation for the Association of Teachers of Technical 
Writing, and then everyone I saw was still reporting maintenance as 
the largest component of software development costs.

> I don't care whether code is readable or not if it does what I want it to.

Doing what you want it to doesn't arise in a vacuum. When tax law 
changes, I want my company's accounting software updated to comply 
with the law; I want this done quickly and I want it done correctly. 
Now the readability of the code is quite important, even if it's 
invisible to me (because the code is owned by someone else).

> If it doesn't do what I want it to I'll get another component that does... 

If one is available, sure; that rather depends on what the market is 
like. And then what you have is competition, and the competitive 
component will be the one that works correctly and represents a good 
value for the cost. That means the developer of that component has 
economic incentive to reduce development cost, and readable code is a 
good way to do that.

> Classes and objects derived from them encapsulate FUNCTIONALITY. Some of it 
> you may write yourself, some of it you may get from people with years of 
> experience you could never hope to acquire. I don't have time (or 
> inclination) to be expert in everything; it is enough to be clear about what 
> I want to achieve and build or buy components that I can use to get there.

Sure. And even before OO became popular, we had a software division of 
labor into operating systems, subsystems, function libraries, 
applications... Even in trivial embedded systems, few people build a 
system from the ground up these days.

But this doesn't change the basic fact that software can be written 
well or poorly. Anyone with experience in the business knows most of 
it isn't written very well, and we stagger along nonetheless. If 
anything, the component market and other forms of commoditizing 
software components (eg SaaS) may finally give software quality some 
decent economic leverage, by increasing transparency in software 
performance.

Ten years ago, if your in-house load balancing failed and a bunch of 
customers couldn't get their requests serviced, you blamed "a computer 
issue" and left it at that. Today, the AWS balancer goes out for a few 
hours and we hear about it on the nightly news. (True, that's partly 
because it was a holiday and it broke Netflix for a lot of customers - 
but that's sort of my point.)

In the commodity software component market, well-written code *may* be 
rewarded, if over time it proves to have an economic advantage. That 
advantage could come from reduced maintenance costs or reduced risk of 
failure or some combination of the two.

> However, it might has well have been Ancient Egyptian when you looked at 
> it... But this comes back to my point above; "readability" depends on the 
> reader. If you were prepared to finance and manage a team of APL experts, it 
> could be a very viable way of running your business. (I wouldn't mind 
> betting there are sites out there that do this...)

There are companies that make extensive use of APL. But as I noted 
above, even APL experts have trouble reading APL, as you can see if 
you read some of the messages on comp.lang.apl.

 > Again, code matters to
> computer scientists. Computer scientists don't run your business.

Depends on your business - there are a lot of "big data" firms out 
there now, for example.

And while code doesn't generally matter to the C-suite, economics 
does. When the CTO says using Brand Y component rather than Brand X 
has reduced downtime by 10% and shortened the average time to get new 
features implemented, that's significant.

> There's a lot of dreadful code in COBOL too. I have looked at and dealt with 
> millions of lines of it, across decades, and across continents. COBOL is far 
> from immune from "bad readability", despite the Holy Mantra to the 
> contrary...

Absolutely.

>> Programming languages certainly affect software development, and some
>> are certainly better than others, particularly in specific domains.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> life easier.

I don't think it is arguable, actually. I don't believe anyone could 
seriously support the assertion that Dartmouth BASIC would be a good 
language to write a real-time OS in, for example. Or that Fortran is 
well-suited for controlling dynamic elements on web pages, or Erlang 
for command-line scripting.

IBM's CL language for the AS/400, circa version 4, had only one 
control structure for iteration: processing each record from a 
database file. If you wanted a loop, of any sort, you had to create a 
database file and write the appropriate number of records into it. 
That's a language "feature" that affected the development of software 
written for it.

>> And while there are a lot
>> more examples and discussions and whatnot for C, say, online than
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> But if it doesn't work, you can easily find an alternative...

Well, no, you can't, in the case of C. The vast majority of C code 
that's available online is terrible. And it's often not obvious to C 
programmers that it's terrible, or what's wrong with it, or even that 
it *is* wrong. It leads to Undefined Behavior, which is defined in the 
C standard as "anything can happen" - including the program working 
correctly, this time. Maybe it'll fail tomorrow; maybe it'll fail in 
ten years.

That's why for a decade or so the majority of security exploits 
documented by organizations like CERT were due to a few types of 
errors in C programs: buffer overrun, format-string vulnerability, 
integer over- and underflow, and failure to check return code. If you 
read the archives of comp.lang.c, you'll find that most of the 
discussion consisted of experts pointing out that questioners had 
written unsafe or undefined code.

So for the non-expert C programmer doing research online, it's very 
difficult to recognize that you're getting bad advice, and even more 
difficult to find good advice.

>> C# is pretty nice, but
>> interest in non-Windows implementations seems to be declining, and the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> hearsay any more than I do...

Regarding non-Windows implementations: Mono is still around (just 
released version 3 in October), but the project has been dropping 
technologies and narrowing its focus. They've dropped Moonlight, the 
open implementation of Silverlight, for example:

http://www.infoq.com/news/2012/05/Miguel-Moonlight

I think there's still a market for Mono and C# on non-Windows 
platforms, and it's looking a bit healthier than it was back when 
Attachmate bought Novell and got rid of a lot of the Mono staff. But 
there's less attention to it in the news than there was four or five 
years ago.

Regarding the political climate at Microsoft: This has been widely 
reported, from a variety of sources. Herb Sutter has been loudly 
proclaiming the ascendancy of native code at Microsoft; see for example:

http://www.theregister.co.uk/2012/11/12/future_of_c_plus_plus/

And while I certainly don't pay much attention to random commentary, 
there are many reasonable analyses that describe the shift toward 
native code in Win8, such as this one:

http://www.guardian.co.uk/technology/blog/2012/feb/17/winrt-windows-8-microsoft-programming

My guess is that this is just the swing of the pendulum; Microsoft put 
a lot of effort into pushing managed code and .Net over the years, and 
now there's a bit of retrenchment just because like most organizations 
they periodically feel the need to change their tune. Combine that 
with the executive-level churn they've had recently, and the resources 
consumed by the creation of WinRT and the various forms of Win8, and 
some flag-waving from the native camp is to be expected. If in a few 
years we see Microsoft rediscovering the wonders of managed code I 
won't be a bit surprised.

> There is a rise in interest in XAML and 
> Javascript and this is being driven by a whole new approach to how we use 
> and develop applications.

Yes - the whole environment around HTML (XAML, Javascript, HTML 5, 
AJAX, etc) is definitely where the attention is focussed right now for 
UI work. And while I could pick nits with any of those, I think that's 
a good way to go for user-facing code.

> The old days of "language wars" are going to become a thing of the past; 
> you'll use tools that will generate code for you and do so in any language 
> that is appropriate.

I don't know that I'd go that far - people will argue about things 
whether they're consequential or not!

 > You'll manipulate functions graphically and connect
> them up and  code will be automatically generated and regenerated in seconds 
> whenever you decide to try your creation.

There will likely be *more* of that, in many domains. (We've had 
visual programming environments like that at least since the '80s; I 
worked on one at IBM, for the Data Explorer/6000 product, toward the 
end of that decade.) I doubt it will ever become universal, because 
programming covers such a wide range of activities.

And someone still has to write those components. Yes, large components 
can be built of smaller ones; but someone developing new low-level 
algorithms isn't going to want to assemble them graphically out of 
primitive components like "take the natural logarithm". (We saw this 
with DX/6000 - at a certain level, users would return to writing code 
directly.)

> It is also important to study business processes and iterative, interactive, 
> development processes (Agile style) too.

Agreed. (For some programming tasks "business processes" may not be 
relevant - for example if you're writing code to crunch scientific 
data - but there will be some equivalent.)


[1] It's one of those mortarboard things.
```

```
On Jan 18, 5:12 am, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:

> This is one area where traditional COBOL's limited modularity really
> hurts it, because it's difficult to create the hierarchy of
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> simple performs for subroutines.

You seem to ignore the COBOL CALL statement. While it was standardised
in '74 it was available in some form much earlier. The dynamic CALL
was significant for several reasons. It catered for creating a
structure for code that gave isolation; along with the CANCEL it gave
memory management (I had multiuser systems on 8085 with 256Kb RAM);
being dynamic it allowed system updates of individual programs
eliminating the need for complete recompiles with associated system
testing.

It also allowed the CALL to use a textfield for the program name which
meant that the system could be very dynamic, it could run different
subprograms based on various parameters or conditions. It was also
possible to structure COBOL programs so that one CALLed subprogram
could perform various functions (methods) depending on the parameters.
For example a 'function' parameter could direct the CALLed subprogram
to execute different code, just as an object performs different
methods.

For example I developed a base system originally CIS COBOL for DRS20
and MP/M which included a file maintenance program that was driven by
text files specifying the file layout and the screen layout. Each file
had its own sub program with the record FD which it would read, write,
rewrite etc depending on the function code passed. It interacted with
a standardised linkage record by moving the fields as required. The
files were designed to make this easy by having only a few variations
of record size and layout into which the data was arranged. Those same
file subprograms were also used in the report program which was driven
by scripts.

The only real restriction on CALL is that there can only be one
instance of each subprogram. If the report program was to use 2 main
files then they could not use the same sub-program, in fact I had to
have a separate subfile program to cater for this. With OO there can
be multiple objects of the same class. However, COBOL systems tend to
be structured in a way that requires serial reuse rather than multiple
objects. Processing an invoice, for example, tends to be done by
reading each invoice line in turn, calculating and printing it and
then moving to the next. This processing is also record orientated so
grouping of data by level numbers is appropriate.
```

Richard wrote:
> On Jan 18, 5:12 am, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:
> 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> You seem to ignore the COBOL CALL statement.

Oh, I'm fully aware of CALL; most of my procedural COBOL uses multiple 
programs and CALL (and in some cases the COBOL 85 support for 
recursion as well) to provide some abstraction, particularly when used 
with multiple source units and contained programs. With some effort 
you can write COBOL that looks like Pascal - not a bad thing.

But in my experience very little COBOL looks like this. Nearly all the 
COBOL source I've seen uses long procedure divisions broken into 
paragraphs (sometimes with section divisions) and uses perform and 
perform-thru as the main control structures. Much of that is due to 
habit, tradition, and the influence of existing code; but COBOL's 
somewhat awkward syntax for subprograms doesn't help.

```
On Wednesday, January 16, 2013 5:42:55 PM UTC-5, Michael Wojcik wrote:

[snip]

> [] Procedural COBOL encourages huge monolithic programs, and its 
> division-based structure creates huge reference problems: definitions 
> far from point of use, flattened hierarchy, lousy encapsulation and 
> abstraction.

"Procedural COBOL encourages ..."? Interesting personification,
but who are the real culprits? The COBOL 85 revision added
EXTERNAL, GLOBAL, nested programs, and EVALUATE. With these
additions it is possible to slice and dice "huge monolithic
programs" into procedural pieces (programs) about the size of
a method. I am not saying it's worthwhile to do so, but it
can be done! However, it might be much better, using features
of COBOL 85, to not create any more "huge monolithic programs".

One can easily go overboard in trying to identify abstractions
for accounting systems. A company that operates a fleet of
trucks and cars probably doesn't need a 'vehicle' abstraction,
since the concrete 'fixed asset' works when supplied with the
appropriate 'depreciation' attribute; and the maintenance cost
for vehicles doesn't appear to fit the abstraction, particularly
when maintenance is not done by the operator of the fleet.
Likewise a manufacturer of trucks and cars would find the
concrete 'bill of materials' to be more useful, because it
includes costs. However, a company producing entertainment
software for children might use a 'vehicle' abstraction to
show how the 'wheels on the bus (truck or car) go round and
round'; but not for their accounting system. My point is that
the primary abstractions for accounting systems are 'procedural
abstractions': 'calculate-depreciation', 'calculate-bom-cost',
'record-sale', etc., and that applies whether OO or procedural
COBOL is used.


> [] Even OO COBOL with simplified syntax (like MF's managed 
> COBOL dialect) still suffers from artifacts of traditional COBOL, such 
> as level numbers (pointless in OO COBOL).

Tables (arrays or matrices) require level numbers in OO COBOL,
even if all other data items could do without.
```

```
On Thursday, January 17, 2013 1:26:40 AM UTC-5, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
> > On Wednesday, January 16, 2013 5:42:55 PM UTC-5, Michael Wojcik wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> Aren't level numbers one of the powerful things about COBOL? []

Yes.

> If you don't use level numbers, don't you need an offset and length into a 
> string or buffer for every field you want to reference? []

Not necessarily in a CLASS definition.

> Am I missing something here?

An implication.

[I need to say things you already know to explain the implication.]

What distinguishes OO COBOL from traditional or procedural COBOL
is the CLASS. In traditional COBOL, level numbers are used to
group related data. A 'person-name' may be defined as:

1 person-name.
 2 last-name ...
 2 first-name ...
 2 middle-initial ...

In a CLASS definition the same data may be defined as:

1 last-name ...
1 first-name ...
1 middle-initial ...

In this example, the level numbers are 'pointless'.

Thus, when Michael referred to level numbers as '(pointless in
OO COBOL)', it was implied (as a necessary consequence) that he
was referring only to CLASS definitions. It follows that my
response was intended to refer only to CLASS definitions.

Furthermore, while COBOL files are another area where level
numbers are used, using instead a RDB with a suitable SQL
preprocessor, VARCHAR items could be transferred to/from
COBOL ANY LENGTH data items eliminating the length/data
structure and the need for these level numbers in a CLASS
definition.

Were it not for tables, I could agree with Michael, but only
within the implicitly limited scope of CLASS definitions.

I hope that clarifies.
```

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-17T11:45:58-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kd99sg314cl@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<alpjt1Fhm7sU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com> <kcpj4j$ecg$1@reader1.panix.com> <cc0446ae-2fe1-4f71-a792-29b8cb66e6e2@googlegroups.com> <09SdnT377JTcmmnNnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com> <kd3m3m02iqc@news3.newsguy.com> <4a35874c-f7c1-4c41-b800-344052896ddc@googlegroups.com> <almdr5Fqo2lU1@mid.individual.net> <kd7aej02tn1@news4.newsguy.com> <c6921a89-7497-4a0d-96be-58abfca08ead@googlegroups.com> <alpjt1Fhm7sU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
>> On Wednesday, January 16, 2013 5:42:55 PM UTC-5, Michael Wojcik wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> Aren't level numbers one of the powerful things about COBOL?

In 1960: yes. In 2013, no.

> The fact that 
> hierarchic data can be easily identified and defined very simply and 
> intuitively has always struck me as a Good Thing...

What problem are you trying to solve?

If it's "a foo object contains a bar object and a baz object", then 
say that the OO way:

class-id bar.
...
end class bar.

class-id baz.
...
end class baz.

class-id foo.
01 bar type bar.
01 baz type baz.
end class foo.

In most modern programming languages, any data type is either a 
primitive type or a class, and a class contains members that are 
primitive types and/or classes. That way everything is strongly typed, 
types are only defined once (so there's no possibility of skew), types 
can have operations (methods) associated with them - all desirable traits.

One of COBOL's faults is that it wants to group things by category, 
rather than by use. Thus we have the data division where all the data 
is defined, followed by a procedure division where all the operations 
on that data are defined. The source code trades locality of reference 
for a sort of consistency that's primarily useful to the compiler. I 
don't organize my hand tools into a toolbox for hammers, one for 
wrenches, and so on; I have a toolbox for plumbing, which contains my 
plumbing tools.

> Although REDEFINES and 
> OCCURS muddy the water when it comes to RDB conversion, they are still 
> pretty powerful for COBOL people.

In place of group items with OCCURS, we use an appropriate container 
generic specialized with an appropriate class. In place of REDEFINES 
we use something sensible, just as we did when we stopped using ALTER.

Of course, when you're converting existing COBOL code, or 
interoperating with it, you may well keep some traditional COBOL data 
definitions. But for new OO COBOL, level numbers are just pointless 
syntactic tics.

(That's not strictly true, from the compiler's point of view. Level 
numbers are currently required for parsing because of potential 
ambiguity with a method that has local data and omits the PROCEDURE 
DIVISION declaration because it doesn't have any parameters. That's 
simply a quirk of the parser, though, and it's easily fixed by having 
an option that requires PROCEDURE DIVISION in exchange for not needing 
level numbers.)
```

I get it now. Thanks to you and Rick for clarifications.
>
> (That's not strictly true, from the compiler's point of view. Level
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> level numbers.)

Pete.

```
Rick Smith wrote:
> On Wednesday, January 16, 2013 5:42:55 PM UTC-5, Michael Wojcik wrote:
> 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> but who are the real culprits?

No personification is necessary. Tools can encourage behaviors through 
their affordances and impedances. There's a mountain of research and 
theory on the subject, from the work of design experts like Donald 
Norman to that of anthropologists like Bruno Latour.

> The COBOL 85 revision added
> EXTERNAL, GLOBAL, nested programs, and EVALUATE. With these
> additions it is possible to slice and dice "huge monolithic
> programs" into procedural pieces (programs) about the size of
> a method.

It's possible even without them (though recursive algorithms are 
difficult to implement, and the lack of parameterization without 
nested programs is an impediment). But there's a tremendous gap 
between what's possible and what's convenient.

> However, it might be much better, using features
> of COBOL 85, to not create any more "huge monolithic programs".

Yes, I think it would. Alas, the COBOL code I see from the field 
mostly still inclines toward the monolithic, and indeed often doesn't 
use many '85 features, much less 2002 ones.

> One can easily go overboard in trying to identify abstractions
> for accounting systems.

Yes, abstraction, like anything else, can be taken too far.

> My point is that
> the primary abstractions for accounting systems are 'procedural
> abstractions': 'calculate-depreciation', 'calculate-bom-cost',
> 'record-sale', etc., and that applies whether OO or procedural
> COBOL is used.

Sure.

>> [] Even OO COBOL with simplified syntax (like MF's managed 
>> COBOL dialect) still suffers from artifacts of traditional COBOL, such 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> even if all other data items could do without.

I assure you they do not; I have thousands of lines of .Net COBOL here 
in front of me with nary a level number, aside from the unfortunately 
necessary 01, and there are plenty of arrays and other containers.

For example:

class-id Vehicle.
01 Vin string.
...
end class Vehicle.

class-id MotorPool.
01 Vehicles type Vehicle occurs any. *> array of vehicles
...
end class MotorPool.

Level numbers become vestigial in OO COBOL because what were group 
items become classes in their own right, and tables become container 
objects holding multiple objects of the appropriate class. Besides 
simple arrays there are all the various sorts of generic containers, 
which offer different trade-offs for different access patterns. In 
.NET, you have List, with fast insertion and deletion and variable 
size; Dictionary, which lets you associate an object and a key; Queue 
and Stack; and so on.

There are some things you can do with level numbers that are 
significantly more complex without them. The obvious example is 
playing with data representation using REDEFINES. But getting away 
from that sort of thing is generally a good idea anyway.
```

```

"Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
news:kd99sf214cl@news7.newsguy.com...
> Rick Smith wrote:
>> On Wednesday, January 16, 2013 5:42:55 PM UTC-5, Michael Wojcik wrote:
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
>
<snip>

IIRC COBOL always supported the CALL statement which could be used to "slice 
and dice".  I always viewed the non-inline PERFORM as a "kind of CALL".
I suppose it is a matter of taste as to whether you prefer the code to be in 
a separate file or as part of a larger program file. An external file makes 
reuse easier without recompiling but by using COPY you can do many of the 
same things.

Part of the monlithic program problem is simply programmer laziness, but 
often requirements lead to programs that are trying to perform too many 
functions.  I used to diagram CALL / PERFORM / XCTL / LINK call trees to 
help me understand large programs.  What I usually found were programs that 
had more depth than width, but some had both.  The only program that I 
recall giving up on diagramming was an accounting program that was very wide 
and very deep.  It processed many different kinds of transactions, often 
with a combination of code shared by other transactions, along with some 
code for specfic differences. This program was simply "too wide" (too many 
different transactions) and also digagramming the depth was highly 
repetitive with only the differences being of any interest.  I simply found 
it unnecessary to diagram the complete program.  This technique has a high 
upfront cost but it allowed me to understand programs that others found too 
dificcult and it paid dividends when revisited the programs for future 
maintainence.  Avoiding use of GO TO can greatly reduce the perceived 
complexity.

One thing I liked about COBOL was that it required the coding of  CALL / 
PERFORM / XCTL / LINK /GO TO.  Because of that and by coding my paragraph 
names in a standard way, it was easy to write a ROSCOE RPF to extract all 
the paragraph names and all their branching statements and merge it all 
together in one file which I then used to determine the programs structure.
```

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> 
> IIRC COBOL always supported the CALL statement which could be used to "slice 
> and dice".

What's supported by a language, and what's used in the culture that 
accretes around that language, are rarely the same thing. It's 
possible to build a large COBOL program from a collection of small, 
specific-purpose subprograms that call one another (though the lack of 
recursion support in older COBOL implementations was a drawback). It's 
rarely done.

COBOL is by no means the only language to have some of its best 
features widely ignored, of course. The vast majority of C programmers 
apparently don't understand critical features like the proper use of 
the sizeof operator, structure initialization, and incomplete 
structure definition, to name only three in that language; the result 
is a vast amount of inferior C code. The same is probably true of most 
languages that see widespread use.

 >  I always viewed the non-inline PERFORM as a "kind of CALL".

It's generally implemented as a subroutine call (with some hidden 
nastiness to support perform ranges) or a non-local branch with a 
hidden return-address storage area, which is nearly a subroutine call. 
But it doesn't allow any parameters (the closure only introduces a 
single binding, for the return address), so it's grossly deficient as 
a control and organization mechanism.

The lack of local parameters means all the parameters have to be 
globals, which increases coupling, decreases cohesion, increases side 
effects, decreases locality of reference - all things that are 
well-established as detrimental to software quality.

COBOL functions are parameterized, and so can be used to some extent 
as a sort of superior non-inline PERFORM, but again I rarely see them 
used in COBOL programs.

The best thing that OO COBOL did was introduce parameterization as a 
matter of course for subroutines (technically methods, but methods are 
just subroutines with frosting). The other OO attributes - better 
aggregate data types, associating operations with data, inheritance, 
polymorphism, etc - are very useful, but parameterization is the 
crucial first step.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 13)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-23T12:48:51+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<am8mr4Fm9hU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com> <kcpj4j$ecg$1@reader1.panix.com> <cc0446ae-2fe1-4f71-a792-29b8cb66e6e2@googlegroups.com> <09SdnT377JTcmmnNnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com> <kd3m3m02iqc@news3.newsguy.com> <4a35874c-f7c1-4c41-b800-344052896ddc@googlegroups.com> <almdr5Fqo2lU1@mid.individual.net> <kd7aej02tn1@news4.newsguy.com> <c6921a89-7497-4a0d-96be-58abfca08ead@googlegroups.com> <kd99sf214cl@news7.newsguy.com> <eYCdnRtxcrwOzmXNnZ2dnUVZ_qSdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <kdm5d6016sg@news6.newsguy.com>`

```
Michael Wojcik wrote:
> Charles Hottel wrote:
>>
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
> just subroutines with frosting).

We diverge here :-)

I don't think the BEST thing OO did was introduce parameterization "as a 
matter of course" and I'll explain why I don't think that below.

I believe encapsulation, inheritance, and having data and behaviours 
packaged together, are all more important than that.


> The other OO attributes - better
> aggregate data types, associating operations with data, inheritance,
> polymorphism, etc - are very useful, but parameterization is the
> crucial first step.

I take no issue with what you say from a CS point of view, Michael. All very 
good points, and true. (Especially the non-locality of reference in COBOL).

Although my initial response here is to Michael, I'm going to explain sme 
stuff that Michael already knows, so please take the rest of this as a 
general response for anyone who is interested...

Parameterization IS important, but way back in the piece (about 15 years 
ago) I gave this a lot of thought and realised the following:

1. If you parameterize method signatures, you are creating a possible pain 
when it has to change.

For example:

I create an object, objX , from Class X and it has a method which takes 4 
parameters:   objx.method1(p1 p2 p3 p4)

This gets invoked by 100 programs.

But now I find that some of these parameters are not required or used by 
some of these programs.  And some of the programs require a 5th parameter, 
but if I add that to the invocation, I have changed the signature of the 
method, so I have to change every one of the 100 programs where it is 
invoked, or create alternate interfaces for it.

We end up with cases where some of the parameters are set to NULL, or 
omitted if the language allows that and it all gets a bit hairy, in my 
opinion.

I want a nice clean method signature that never changes and is consistent 
for the life of the component. I want to know that if I require the 
functionality of method1 of objX, I can just instantiate objX and invoke 
method1. (objX.method1).

2. Like I said, when I was first coming to grips with OOP, I gave this a 
good deal of thought. It seemed to me that parameters are really just ways 
of modifying a fundamental behaviour so that it can be more generalised. I 
saw two solutions:

1. Write a different Class method for each parameter or combination of 
parameters and set properties within each method before invoking it.
2.  Let the parameters be Class (Factory) or Method Properties and eliminate 
parameter passing altogether. (2 actually derived from 1, because 1 was not 
at all an attractive proposition.)

So, instead of passing parameters in and out of an object method, just SET 
the parameters as Properties before invoking the method, then GET the 
Properties after invoking the method. This way, the invoker only deals with 
the specific parameters it is interested in to modify the method's 
behaviour, and the "parameterization" is removed completely. Method 
signature is clean and minimal and there is no problem with remembering it 
or getting it wrong, or passing unused or null parameters back and forth. 
Each invoker knows what it wants and it deals with the set up for that 
particular invocation by SETting the appropriate Properties, invoking the 
method, then GETting the results if it needs them. (In some cases, the 
invoker is not really interested in the invoked process, just seeing that it 
has happened so something further down the line has what it needs.).

This approach has worked very well for me using component based design. I 
don't parameterize methods unless it is a very simple set of parameters that 
are ALWAYS likely to be needed.

I DO create within the method public Properties that would have previously 
been passed parameters.

(I have carried the same principles into my C# development and it works very 
well. C# supports the GETting and SETting of properties with simple dot 
syntax and the setup of public Properties within a Class has been 
abbreviated to a single line in the later versions of the language.)

Compare:
 (COBOL):
01   MyProperty   pic x(256) PROPERTY. *> a public property which should be 
set by the caller...

Very nice, one line definition of a public Property in a Class/Method.

("early C#"):
        private static string _MyProperty;
        public static string MyProperty    {
            get
            {
                return _MyProperty;
            }
            set
            {
                _MyProperty = value;
            }
        }

Kludgy 11 line definition of a Property. Although it shows the detail of 
what happens, we are not really interested in that. We just want our 
Property to be safe...

("current  C#"):
public string MyProperty { get; set; } // a public property which should be 
set by the caller...

One line definition as for COBOL. (The private static property is subsumed 
under the covers, so it is still "safe"...)

However, the crunch comes when we need to GET or SET the Property.... Here's 
the respective code in the invoking programs:

COBOL (Fujitsu):

      INVOKE  objX  "SET-MyProperty" *> SETs object property to value of 
ws-MyProperty
          USING  ws-MyProperty
      END-INVOKE

      INVOKE  objX  "GET-MyProperty" *> GETs object property into 
ws-MyProperty
          RETURNING  ws-MyProperty
      END-INVOKE

... and, in C#:

objX.MyProperty = ws-MyProperty;      //SETs object property to value of 
ws-MyProperty
   ws-MyProperty = objX.MyProperty;   //GETs object property into 
ws-MyProperty

(This also demonstrates my argument, made elsewhere, that once COBOL guys 
start understanding OOP they move away from COBOL...
  The C# is just as "readable" in terms of clarity and it takes much less 
writing. Apart from reducing RSI in programmers, if you write less you can 
do it quicker... :-))

If a new parameter has to be added, I can add a new Property to the Class 
and ONLY the invokers that need the new parameter are affected. No 
regression testing is required; I can just test the ones that use the new 
property and be assured that the ones that don't will continue running as 
they aways have. (If the new parameter was passed through the method 
signature, it might be necessary to amend and recompile all 100 invoking 
programs...). Of course, if some funny things started happening after the 
new Property was added, THEN it might be necessary to regression test and 
debug; so far, in 15 years, I have never had to do that.

When I first came up with this I was pretty excited and thought I had 
discovered something that could revolutionize the maintenance of program 
code... :-) (I was still pretty much in COBOL mode... :-))

Since then I have read numerous papers discussing the relative benefits of 
GET and SET and I realize that the computer scientists were way ahead of 
me... :-)

Never mind, I arrived at it on my own and it works extremely well for me. 
:-)

Pete.
```

```
On 23 Jan, 12:48, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:

> Although my initial response here is to Michael, I'm going to explain sme
> stuff that Michael already knows, so please take the rest of this as a
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
> invoked, or create alternate interfaces for it.

No you don't, not in real OO such as C++. You just use overloading and
add a method of the same name with a different set of parameters. In C+
+ the name mangling sorts out which of the several methods of the same
name to invoke. This new method can invoke the old method.

Given:
     int crunch (int a) ...

you can add:
     int crunch (int a, int b) ...
         this.b = b
         this.crunch(a)

and then programs can have either whatsit.crunch(mya) or
whatsit.crunch(mya, myb)


> We end up with cases where some of the parameters are set to NULL, or
> omitted if the language allows that and it all gets a bit hairy, in my
> opinion.

Python doesn't have overloading but has named parameters with defaults
so it is only necessary to change

     def crunch(self, a):
to
     def crunch(self, a, b=0, testing=False, ratio=1.0):

Callers could then use, for example:
     whatsit.crunch(mya, ratio=1.2, testing=True)
or
     whatsit.crunch(mya)
or
     whatsit.crunch(mya, 1, True, 1.2)

Existing programs need not be changed.


> I want a nice clean method signature that never changes and is consistent
> for the life of the component. I want to know that if I require the
> functionality of method1 of objX, I can just instantiate objX and invoke
> method1. (objX.method1).

Overloading does that. Parameter defaults do that too.

> 2. Like I said, when I was first coming to grips with OOP, I gave this a
> good deal of thought. It seemed to me that parameters are really just ways
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> has happened so something further down the line has what it needs.).

Congratulations, you just invented the 1960s.

      MOVE MY_A      TO CALC_A
      MOVE MY_B      TO CALC_B
      PERFORM 7423_CALC_C
      MOVE CALC_RESULT TO MY_RESULT

> This approach has worked very well for me using component based design. I
> don't parameterize methods unless it is a very simple set of parameters that
…[85 more quoted lines elided]…
> me... :-)

Do you mean like this one:

"""There are actually many good reasons to consider using accessors
rather than directly exposing fields of a class - beyond just the
argument of encapsulation and making future changes easier.

Here are the some of the reasons I am aware of:

    Encapsulation of behavior associated with getting or setting the
property - this allows additional functionality (like validation) to
be added more easily later.
    Hiding the internal representation of the property while exposing
a property using an alternative representation.
    Insulating your public interface from change - allowing the public
interface to remain constant while the implementation changes without
effecting existing consumers.
    Controlling the lifetime and memory management (disposal)
semantics of the property - particularly important in non-managed
memory environments (like C++ or Objective-C).
    Providing a debugging interception point for when a property
changes at runtime - debugging when and where a property changed to a
particular value can be quite difficult without this in some
languages.
    Improved interoperability with libraries that are designed to
operate against property getter/setters - Mocking, Serialization, and
WPF come to mind.
    Allowing inheritors to change the semantics of how the property
behaves and is exposed by overriding the getter/setter methods.
    Allowing the getter/setter to be passed around as lambda
expressions rather than values.
    Getters and setters can allow different access levels - for
example the get may be public, but the set could be protected.
"""
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 16)_

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-23T12:43:35-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9f1c8e94-2ce0-40a9-b2f1-31544099d203@i2g2000pbi.googlegroups.com>`

```
On 24 Jan, 08:13, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
wrote:
> Richard wrote:
> > On 23 Jan, 12:48, "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
…[235 more quoted lines elided]…
> stated.

It took me 5 seconds with Google to find that C# (and Ada) supports
named parameters and optional arguments for the last dozen years or
so.

 http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/dd264739.aspx

Your reasoning for avoiding method parameters is very flawed. You are
using an obsolete mechanism (cf 1960s cobol perform) to avoid a
problem that never existed (cf overloading) and has several better
solutions (cf default arguments).

The basis of your argument: "so I have to change every one of the 100
programs where it is invoked, or create alternate interfaces for it."
is simply not true and is the _reason_ for using OO rather than non-
OO. Your 'solution' may have been appropriate one for old C modules*
or COBOL performs but it avoids the features that OO created to
provide for that exact problem.


> Mainly, I have found it meets my needs so I don't really mind if there are
> alternatives I may have missed.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> that's why I shared it :-))

I know that it is too late to change your mind, but others may read
these messages.



* In C (in the 1980s) one could have libraries with extern variables.
These could be used to act as public attributes so a library could be
created with public and private variables and public and private
(static) functions (methods). In fact one could create an extern
struct to contain the public area and use this to create the
namespace. Thus one could 'get' and 'set' the module data items
without using parameters. Then, as you do, you can avoid changing
programs that call the module when data requirement change. Doing it
in an OO language simply avoids making it OO at all.
```

Richard wrote:

> in an OO language simply avoids making it OO at all.

Parameters, or lack of them are not what makes something OO. There are MANY 
other benefits to using an OO approach but there is little point in arguing 
it.

In my opinion, having NO parameters is better than relying on name mangling 
with overloading, or default parameters, or named parameters, or  using 
external parameters, or any parameters being passed at all.

(there are some occasions where I DO use parameters to methods but they are 
trivial.)

As for the approach being 1960s, that is a matter of opinion too and even if 
you are right, it doesn't matter, as long as the approach is useful and 
gives the claimed benefits.

Your arguments, while interesting, have not persuaded me to rush out and 
change all my code :-) and as for "other people watching", I'm sure they can 
make their own decision as to what they would prefer.

I posted my experience, not because I think it is perfect and everyone 
should emulate it, but because it MIGHT be of use to somebody even if it 
just makes them think about the implications of using parameters. Whatever 
else it may be, it is simple and straightforward. My experience has been 
that approaches with those attributes are usually "better".

Pete.

```
On Jan 23, 12:48 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

[...]

> ... and, in C#:
>
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> ws-MyProperty

[...]

> If a new parameter has to be added, I can add a new Property to the Class
> and ONLY the invokers that need the new parameter are affected. No
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> debug; so far, in 15 years, I have never had to do that.

It occured to me that there is another problem with your mechanism and
that is when you have added a new property which would be used in a
calculation. For example if the existing code was:

     objX.value1 = 1
     objX.value2 = 2
     objX.someCalc()
     result = objX.calculatedResult

but then decided that sometimes you needed to have another factor. For
example this calculated some aerodynamics and for models and Bumble
Bees you needed the Reynolds Number which was not required for the
first 100 programs that had been written. So you add in the class:

     this.ReynoldsNumber = 1.0

and for models:

     objX.value1 = 1
     objX.value2 = 2
     objX.ReynoldsNumber = 1.2
     objX.someCalc()
     result = objX.calculatedResult

That then works fine and Bumble Bees _can_ fly after all.

But if you then have a mixed program made from existing modules and
this new one then after a model call the ReynoldsNumber in the object
will be left as 1.2 and this will not be reset by the old programs.

Using a default argument overcomes this:

     def float someCalc(val1, val2, ReynoldsNumber=1.0)

means that if the 3rd parameter is omitted, which is what the existing
programs do, then the correct default value will be used.

One of the advantages of using parameters is that the compiler can
check them and warn, or error, if the wrong number or types are used.
Your mechanism avoids these checks.
```

Richard wrote:

If I needed a Reynolds number (sometimes) I would add a Reynolds number 
property to the Class and  SET it on invocation. Anything that used it 
(whether model or bumblebee) would set it before doing so, just as the 
properties to be used are set by each invocation. There will be no "residual 
value" and even if there was, it wouldn't matter because anything that was 
actually going to use it would reset it before doing so.

> Your mechanism avoids these checks.

Yes, it does. As I ALWAYS late bind everything, I have to take care that I 
get this right myself.  The "type and number of Properties" can NEVER be 
wrong; there is nothing being passed directly. If a GET or SET tries to use 
an inappropriate value an exception is thrown and I hear about it PDQ. Once 
programs move out of debugging I have never had this problem occur.

Like I said before, it is simple and straightforward.

But, apart from that, it works very well and has caused me no grief in 15 
years.

Pete.

```
Sorry to take so long to respond. My first grandchild was born over 
the weekend, and my wife and I were along for the ride (the kid lives 
about half a mile from us), so I've been otherwise engaged. Things 
should settle down again soon, though - probably in about eighteen years.


Pete Dashwood wrote:
> 
> 1. If you parameterize method signatures, you are creating a possible pain 
> when it has to change.

Sure. This is the standard coupling problem. OO languages typically 
try to solve this with polymorphic methods (or inheritance, when the 
changes are more extensive), so you can extend the API without 
altering existing code.

> 1. Write a different Class method for each parameter or combination of 
> parameters and set properties within each method before invoking it.
> 2.  Let the parameters be Class (Factory) or Method Properties and eliminate 
> parameter passing altogether. (2 actually derived from 1, because 1 was not 
> at all an attractive proposition.)

This isn't eliminating parameterization; it's encapsulating it. Since 
object methods all have a hidden parameter that represents the object 
that's being acted on (the "this" or "self" parameter), you've simply 
encapsulated the other parameters in the object itself.

Often this is a good technique. Instead of identifying parameters by 
position, it in effect identifies them by name - which means 
additional metadata is available to avoid certain classes of bugs. It 
lets parameters persist across methods, which can reduce source-code 
duplication, a common source of errors. And, per your original point, 
it makes the API more flexible and easy to extend.

It also has drawbacks. The first main one is that the compiler can't 
automatically detect missing parameters (since it doesn't know what 
properties the method expects to have set) - if you want contract 
enforcement, you have to implement that manually. The other is that 
sometimes a parameter doesn't logically "belong" to the object that's 
being invoked, so making it a property is incongruous. (Some people 
would refer to this as a "code smell".)

But this technique can be very valuable. It's especially useful in 
dynamic OO languages, particularly allotyped ones, where you can 
assign any property to any object and create types on the fly. Some 
Javascript[1] programmers habitually specify anonymous objects as 
method parameters, like this:

	thing.setColor({blue: 55, red: 123, green: 97});

Then the "setColor" method is written to take a single parameter and 
look for properties named "blue", "red", and "green" in it. It could 
be an on-the-fly anonymous object like the one in the example, or some 
other object that happens to contain those properties.



[1] Actually ECMAScript, of which Javascript is one implementation. 
But nearly everyone uses the wrong name.

> However, the crunch comes when we need to GET or SET the Property.... Here's 
> the respective code in the invoking programs:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> ws-MyProperty

In MF COBOL:

	set objX::MyProperty to ws-MyProperty
	set ws-Myproperty to objX::MyProperty


>   The C# is just as "readable" in terms of clarity and it takes much less 
> writing. Apart from reducing RSI in programmers, if you write less you can 
> do it quicker... :-))

I would still argue that this is a false economy. If actual programmer 
productivity - in terms of new functionality implemented or quality 
improved - is significantly limited by the number of characters your 
programmers have to type, then there is something SEVERELY wrong with 
your development process.

Actually writing code - adding characters to source code - probably 
shouldn't even occupy most of a programmer's time. Much better to 
spend that time on design, review, prefactoring, and other activities 
aimed at producing high-quality, expressive code from the outset.

> Since then I have read numerous papers discussing the relative benefits of 
> GET and SET and I realize that the computer scientists were way ahead of 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> :-)

Nothing wrong with discovering techniques by trying things out!
```

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>> I would still argue that this is a false economy. If actual programmer
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> you are looking for?

I agree when there's a substantial difference in the number of lines 
of code. While this will differ a bit among readers, clearly the 
implementation of a particular piece of functionality can be too short 
(as can be the case with something like APL, or excessively-clever C) 
or too long for optimal readability.

> I would rather have 30 lines of C# (even if it was "complex") than 300 lines 
> of COBOL, if I have to find and correct a problem., but I accept not 
> everyone would agree. My take on it is that if it is "small" it can be 
> rewritten from scratch (if the need arises), within the same timeframe that 
> you would spend combing through it if it was "larger".

Sure - though if there's a order-of-magnitude difference between the 
two implementations, that may be a sign that the longer version could 
use some refactoring. Or it could be that one version is making much 
more use of external functionality - libraries, frameworks, components 
- so the comparison is not so much between the languages as it is 
between the execution environments.

If you're going to compare C# and COBOL, for example, it's only fair 
to compare C# to a .NET COBOL, even if it's procedural COBOL. (It 
would hardly be fair to rule the C# a failure because you can't run it 
on the mainframe; not letting COBOL use the .NET Framework is just as 
arbitrary.) And in that case there's simply no reason for the COBOL to 
be ten times as long as the C#. Of course someone might write it that 
way anyway...
```
