```
On Jan 18, 5:33 am, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
> > On Wednesday, January 16, 2013 5:42:55 PM UTC-5, Michael Wojcik wrote:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> between what's possible and what's convenient.

It is not necessary to use nested programs for parameters, external
programs work quite nicely even with '74. Generally the parameters
were 01 level records with several data items. These could be extended
where necessary _without_ having to recompile everything as long as
the called program used some signal (such as size of) to know when
additional items could be used or not.

Recursion is never actually needed, iteration can be used with equal
effect, it is simply a matter of maintaining your 'stack' in an array.
I did write a quicksort for arrays, which is usually recursive, in CIS
COBOL which is/was '74. It was used initially for machines with 8"
diskettes and slow 8" hard drives (so writing the array out to disk
and using SORT was far too slow) but the code was still used this
century. On an 8085 or even a 386 it was faster than a brute force
nested perform.

I have also done interactive programs which emulated recursive
windowing but it did require an EXTERNAL block to coordinate the
dozens of mutually called programs, each with one window (dialog box).
This did provide a benefit in not allowing hugely deep z orders.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-28T11:07:34-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ke67qb126fr@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<8016e00f-b7ca-4596-99bd-3d33be07d4ec@i2g2000pbi.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com> <kcpj4j$ecg$1@reader1.panix.com> <cc0446ae-2fe1-4f71-a792-29b8cb66e6e2@googlegroups.com> <09SdnT377JTcmmnNnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com> <kd3m3m02iqc@news3.newsguy.com> <4a35874c-f7c1-4c41-b800-344052896ddc@googlegroups.com> <almdr5Fqo2lU1@mid.individual.net> <kd7aej02tn1@news4.newsguy.com> <c6921a89-7497-4a0d-96be-58abfca08ead@googlegroups.com> <kd99sf214cl@news7.newsguy.com> <8016e00f-b7ca-4596-99bd-3d33be07d4ec@i2g2000pbi.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard wrote:
> On Jan 18, 5:33 am, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:
>> Rick Smith wrote:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> programs work quite nicely even with '74.

Yes. I edited a longer remark about the lack of parameterization with 
PERFORM, and the relative awkwardness of using external programs for 
small functions, down into a single comment that as it stands is 
incorrect. Ideally, you want to be able to decompose processing into 
small pieces that perform a single, coherent function (these may be 
called "subroutines" or "functions" or "methods" or whatever). When a 
bunch of these are related, it's convenient to be able to edit them 
together, in a single unit of source (source file or source member or 
whatever it may be).

There are in concept other ways of organizing source code - the "file" 
metaphor has been with us for an awfully long time, and there have 
been attempts to organize and view the source hierarchy in other ways. 
But for the most part we're stuck with files, and not being able to 
put a bunch of related small, parameterized routines in the same file 
makes it inconvenient to create modular programs.

> Recursion is never actually needed, iteration can be used with equal
> effect, it is simply a matter of maintaining your 'stack' in an array.

I didn't say it was needed. In fact there's a theorem which proves, by 
construction, that any recursive algorithm can be transformed into an 
iterative one with a fixed stack requirement, regardless of problem 
size. (In other words, the "maintain your own stack" approach is 
really just manually implementing recursion, and there are more 
sophisticated ways to eliminate recursion.) I said recursive 
algorithms are "difficult to implement" without support for recursion 
in the language, and in general I believe that's the case, though of 
course what counts as "difficult" is subjective. Certainly it's more 
trouble than letting the language do the work for you.

> I did write a quicksort for arrays, which is usually recursive, in CIS
> COBOL which is/was '74.

Iterative Quicksort is relatively straightforward; the Standard C 
Library's qsort is often implemented as an iterative Quicksort. 
(qsort, despite its name, need not be Quicksort at all - the standard 
only imposes a few requirements on it.) If you limit the size of the 
input (and in practice it'll be limited by address space, for an 
in-memory sort), you can do it with a fixed number of "stack frames" 
and avoid dynamic allocation. In fact, with some clever coding, you 
can ensure that you'll never need more than N frames to sort up to 
2**N records - so, for example, you can implement iterative Quicksort 
with an array of 32 frames for 32-bit programs.

Simple self-recursive algorithms can be transformed with this sort of 
keep-your-own-stack logic, mostly a matter of writing housekeeping 
code. Things get more complicated with mutually-recursive algorithms 
or cases of indirect recursion where your call graph has cycles but 
they're not trivial. Those do show up in the real world.

For example, I have a Telnet server implementation written in COBOL - 
fortunately in OO COBOL, with support for recursion. During the Telnet 
negotiation process, it's quite common to start processing a Telnet 
command sequence from the peer, call a subroutine that handles that 
particular command, and while processing the command data find an 
embedded Telnet command sequence. The cleanest way to process that is 
to call the Telnet-command subroutine, which is already on the call 
stack, so you have indirect recursion.

It's a question of affordance. Older COBOLs did not make it impossible 
to write modular programs, but they offered more resistance to, and 
less encouragement for, modularity than later languages (including 
later COBOLs) did. Consequently, COBOL developed a population of 
programmers and a culture that favored monolithic programs with 
relatively high coupling (side effects in one part of the program can 
affect distant parts) and low cohesion (the program isn't broken up 
into "modules" that each have a relatively narrow, specific purpose).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 13)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-29T01:46:15-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

Rick Smith wrote:
> On Monday, January 28, 2013 11:07:34 AM UTC-5, Michael Wojcik wrote:
> 
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> parameterized routines in the same file".

I was under the impression that multiple top-level programs in the 
same source file (a compilation group containing multiple source 
units, in the language of the COBOL 2002 standard) were only allowed 
as of COBOL '85. If they were allowed in COBOL '74, then that does 
provide a way of creating modular, parameterized COBOL programs. It's 
still a bit awkward in some ways, but most of those can be addressed 
through other means (eg copybooks to define global data items in each 
of the programs).

On Wednesday, January 30, 2013 8:50:26 AM UTC-5, Michael Wojcik wrote:
> Rick Smith wrote:
> > 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> encouraged writing monolithic programs.

Not quite! It began with Pete's other languages do OO better.
In the course of responding-"'Better' is debatable."-you wrote,
"Procedural COBOL encourages huge monolithic programs, and its
division-based structure creates huge reference problems:
definitions far from point of use, flattened hierarchy, lousy
encapsulation and abstraction." No distinction was made regarding
any version of standard COBOL, though OO implied 2002, in context.

I objected to 'encourages' and, for the record, I prefer
'influences', based on William A. Wulf's "implicit Whorfian
hypothesis".[1]

Then ...
> On 16 January you pointed out that COBOL 85 introduced features such 
> as EXTERNAL and GLOBAL, making it possible to write modular programs. 
> On the 17th I replied that it's possible even without those features, 
> but inconvenient, for various reasons; and the conversation continued 
> from there.

Richard brought up COBOL 74, but I did not varying from 2002
and the still often used 85. Thus, when you mentioned multiple
source units in the present tense, I supplied an example from
COBOL 85 that still applies to 2002. When you responded, mentioning
COBOL 74, it could not have referred to anything I said or implied.


-----
[1] William A. Wulf, "Trends in the Design and Implementation of
Programming Languages", Computer [IEEE], January 1980.

"There is an implicit Whorfian hypothesis that the nature of
language shapes and molds the ways in which we think about
problems. Thus, although we may not be able to measure it
directly, most experts believe that the user of one of the
more modern, structured languages is better equipped to think
about complex problems than the user of one of the older
languages (e.g., Fortran)."

```
On Jan 30, 8:51 am, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:

> I was under the impression that multiple top-level programs in the
> same source file (a compilation group containing multiple source
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> of the programs).

I am not sure why you think that the 'same source file' is important.
Whether or not there are multiple programs in one source file is an
issue for the compiler and/or the development environment. We could do
batch compilations on ANS'61 compiler by stacking the source code card
decks together, or later from the source code mag tape.

ANS'85 did introduce the 'END PROGRAM' as a standard but previous
compilers could, and did, use different separators, such as ****
(which was the typical card terminator on ICL1900 series).

Parameterized programs were perfectly usable with each program in a
separate source file. Multi-file editors and 'make', or similar, were
all that was required to tie the modules together. The only advantage
of a single file was if you didn't have a multi-file editor* or if you
needed to start each compilation manually.

* Actually back on CP/M I didn't have a multi-file editor but I did
have vfile which was a file manager that could fire off the editor on
the selected file so it wasn't like I needed to use the command line.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Getting started with Cobol 2002 +

_(thread depth: 16)_

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2013-01-30T09:05:32-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kebaau1cj6@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<bbbd8969-6359-4fe6-be1a-f086bf568793@s8g2000pbw.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<972f1c4e-91d0-4002-88f1-660e39526e7f@googlegroups.com> <kcpj4j$ecg$1@reader1.panix.com> <cc0446ae-2fe1-4f71-a792-29b8cb66e6e2@googlegroups.com> <09SdnT377JTcmmnNnZ2dnUVZ_vKdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <fc088cdc-b713-44a0-bb16-3f0b5acfab06@googlegroups.com> <kd3m3m02iqc@news3.newsguy.com> <4a35874c-f7c1-4c41-b800-344052896ddc@googlegroups.com> <almdr5Fqo2lU1@mid.individual.net> <kd7aej02tn1@news4.newsguy.com> <c6921a89-7497-4a0d-96be-58abfca08ead@googlegroups.com> <kd99sf214cl@news7.newsguy.com> <8016e00f-b7ca-4596-99bd-3d33be07d4ec@i2g2000pbi.googlegroups.com> <ke67qb126fr@news3.newsguy.com> <ea1a3609-33e4-4d97-a0fe-917574556298@googlegroups.com> <ke997s1242o@news7.newsguy.com> <bbbd8969-6359-4fe6-be1a-f086bf568793@s8g2000pbw.googlegroups.com>`

```
Richard wrote:
> On Jan 30, 8:51 am, Michael Wojcik <mwoj...@newsguy.com> wrote:
> 
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> I am not sure why you think that the 'same source file' is important.

It's a habitual unit of work for the programmer. A majority of 
development environments - from punching card decks to vi running in a 
Unix shell to ISPF to the first workstation IDEs - have had 
programmers work in source files or members of some sort. That's what 
the programmer has to hand when reading or editing a program; so it 
becomes natural (in the sense of unexamined, without further thought) 
to treat a source file as a functional unit.

Some languages even enshrine this division, as Java did with its "one 
(top-level) class per file" organization. Others, such as C, use 
source file divisions to control visibility; this originated in the 
simple linkage process available to early C implementations, but was 
carried forward into the language standard for reasons of compatibility.

That makes programmers strongly inclined to let the source-file 
organization reflect the organization of the program as a whole. 
Grouping associated pieces of functionality into source files helps 
keep the program structure aligned to the programmer's mental model of 
the program's architecture.

Also, in many environments (particularly before the advent of the 
modern IDE and "messy-desk" windowing, both of which support viewing 
an arbitrary number of files), switching between source files is 
significantly more work than remaining within a single source file. So 
in those environments, aspects of the programmer's workflow - reading 
and editing code - penalize breaking related functionality up among 
multiple source files.
```

There's a precompiled Windows version of OpenCOBOL available for free 
download.

It can be a little confusing navigating their website:
http://www.opencobol.org/

But if you get to this page:

http://www.opencobol.org/modules/bwiki/index.php?Assorted%20Documents

Down near the bottom of look for:

OpenCOBOL-1.1-06FEB2009-mingw.zip

"WINDOWS USERS - - - Want to get started QUICKLY with OpenCOBOL? This is 
just what you need. This 26Mb Windows ZIP file contains a complete 
OpenCOBOL 1.1 installation that's ready-to-run. It was built from the 
06FEB2009 OC 1.1 distribution using the MinGW/MSYS Unix-emulator. It was 
built using Berkeley Database V5.0.26, GMP V5.0.1 and PDCurses V4.3 
(with OC patches to enable all the color syntax to function perfectly).

The EXEs and DLLs have been run on Windows XP, Windows Vista and Windows 
7 systems using both AMD and Intel processors with both 32-bit and 
64-bit operating systems.

Everything you need is there in the ZIP file, including all the other 
downloads on this page. After you download the file, read the 
OpenCOBOL-1.1-06FEB2009-MinGW-Distribution-README.PDF file for 
instructions on "installing" this distribution - five minutes after your 
download finishes, you're in business!"

That distribution was last updated in December, 2010.

http://www.mediafire.com/file/v943ibx2p89osdp/OpenCOBOL-1.1-06FEB2009-mingw.zip

## Thread: Free COBDATA Tool on COBOL21 site — 5 msgs

### Free COBDATA Tool on COBOL21 site

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-16T22:45:43+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<alnb69F20ipU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I was informed today that the COBDATA tool at: .

http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21

...has not  been working for some downloads

The problem was because it was not intended to run on 64 bit platforms.

If anyone has tried to download and use this tool on a 64 bit platform, 
please accept my apologies. The web site should have been clear it was a 32 
bit implementation only, but at the time we built it there were not so many 
64 bit systems around.

Anyway, I have fixed it and it should now be downloadable to 32 and 64 bit 
platforms from Windows XP onwards. (I can't guarantee Win 98...)

Pete.
```

- **Date:** 2013-01-19T11:41:22-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1iflf8t4n6s9pk7vhnhcjpcncld3k4j94t@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<alnb69F20ipU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Wed, 16 Jan 2013 22:45:43 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Free COBDATA Tool on COBOL21 site

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-20T13:58:38+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<am0tq0F7ielU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<alnb69F20ipU1@mid.individual.net> <1iflf8t4n6s9pk7vhnhcjpcncld3k4j94t@4ax.com>`

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
> On Wed, 16 Jan 2013 22:45:43 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> installation magic is done?

The "installation magic" can't do anything about a 32 bit program that tries 
to  load a 64 bit library... :-)

The COBDATA application has COBOL and C# components  in it. (The source is 
downloadable from COBOL21)

The C# was built to run on "AnyCPU". When it is installed on a 64 bit 
system, it runs as a 64 bit application. However, the COBOL uses a 32 bit 
native runtime. As soon as the COBOL tries to call C# it crashes and 
burns...

By rebuilding the C# to target a 32 bit processor, everything runs 
correctly. (It is using 32 bit code irrespective of the processor so it runs 
on both 32 and 64 bit platforms.)

Most people have no idea of the complexity which is under the covers to 
allow your new 64 bit platform to run 32 bit applications. It is really 
quite amazing but it does require some consideration from developers if they 
are using mixed languages across platforms. Some developers actually build 
two versions and detect at install time which one to install.

C# on its own would be fine, if it is built as targeting "AnyCPU". It will 
run as managed 32 or 64 bit code depending on the processor, and the CIL is 
appropriately JIT compiled to the right CLR and loaded at run time. It 
doesn't require "installation" or any other "magic" :-)

Unfortunately for me, the COBOL legacy is a millstone round  my neck that 
ties everything to 32 bit, even though it can run on a 64 bit platform, 
thanks to OS support from Windows.

If I was prepared to invest in a new CIL generating COBOL compiler (which I 
am not... they are outrageously expensive when CIL generating compilers for 
other languages (C#, VB.Net...) are free... and I am moving away from COBOL 
anyway, so it is a hard to pill to swallow further investment into it...) 
then I could create true 64 bit applications and components and the problem 
wouldn't arise.

The bottom line for me is that anything I use that has legacy components in 
it has to be targeted for "x86" (32 bit) architecture. Largely for that 
reason, I am upping the priority on converting the remaining COBOL 
components to C#, but it is a large amount of work and has some risks 
attached.

My mixed language applications CAN run on 32 or 64 bit, but the only way I 
will unleash the full power of 64 bit is to remove the COBOL.

It may be worth noting in passing that 32 bit applications running on 64 bit 
platforms will suffer a slight decrease in performance. (mostly undetectable 
by humans). This is a bit counter-intuitive (you might expect a more 
powerful processor to run faster; it does, but this is offset by extra work 
it has to do to allow your 32 bit stuff to execute...). On the few 
applications I have developed to test the new platforms, there is a 
noticeable increase in performance when 64 bit apps run on 64 bit platforms. 
A particular test where I ran identical functionality developed as 32 and 64 
bit managed code, showed around 15% better in 64 bit. However, you can't 
infer much from a sample of one, and it is just an indication.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Free COBDATA Tool on COBOL21 site

- **From:** Waldek Hebisch <hebisch@math.uni.wroc.pl>
- **Date:** 2013-01-21T02:17:08+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<kdi8f4$mup$1@z-news.wcss.wroc.pl>`
- **References:** `<alnb69F20ipU1@mid.individual.net> <1iflf8t4n6s9pk7vhnhcjpcncld3k4j94t@4ax.com> <am0tq0F7ielU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> 
> It may be worth noting in passing that 32 bit applications running on 64 bit 
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> infer much from a sample of one, and it is just an indication.

This is badly worded: 32 bit code running on 64-bit processor will
run as fast as on similar 32-bit processor.  In fact, if you code
the _same_ instructions as 32-bit or 64-bit, then 32-bit version
will be faster.  On CPU-intensive code 64-bit version should
be faster (of order 10-20%) becase 64-bit mode has new features
(mainly more registers) and uses more efficient software
conventions (32-bit mode is stuck with less efficient conventions
to keep backwards compatibility).  On memory intensive code
32-bit version frequently will win because nomally 32-bit data
takes less space and consequently there is less data to move
then in 64-bit version (but there are exceptions: I have a program
where data items nicely fit in 64-bit words, but need complex
data structure (bigger than 64-bits) in 32-bit mode).

Anyway, if the 32-bit programs run slower than 64-bit ones it
is normally not due some inefficiency of 64-bit system in
handling 32-bit code, rather it due to extra features available
in 64-bit mode.  Or, if you prefer, inefficiency is in the
32-bit instructions, not in the processor.

I wrote "normally" above because there is some inefficency in
mixing 32-bit and 64-bit code: you need to sets of libraries,
which takes more memory.  But on modern systems this is unlikely
to have measurable impact on performance.
```

<a id="t-5b93cc5ea1"></a>
## Thread: OT: Anti-virus conclusions — 4 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: Anti-virus conclusions

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-21T18:55:12+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<am43i1Ft5hvU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I have now completed weeks of my own testing on various anti-virus programs.

The main conclusion:

"Don't believe what they say... the ONLY real test is how it runs on YOUR 
systems..."

I won't go into all the tests I did or what products I did them on because 
some people are likely to get completely different results and it isn't 
really fair for my opinions to possibly cloud someone else's experience.

What I CAN say is:

1. Some products scan much quicker than others.
2. Some products install much quicker than others.
3. Some products install much easier than others.
4. Sadly, too many of these systems just will not uninstall properly. I 
already mentioned Norton which simply destroyed the entire system when an 
attempt was made to uninstall it, but they are not alone...
5. Some of these systems (written in C or C++) distribute incompatible C 
runtimes that mean that exceptions in the processing are not handled and the 
results are less than you would want. (Frequent popups complaining about 
missing entry points in the C runtime library  (msvcrt32.dll) don't inspire 
confidence in a product...)
6. After having to "hand wash" the registry on at least 2 occasions, not to 
mention usng a very good Registry cleaner on others, I have come to the 
conclusion that some of these products should be banned... It is no fun 
going through 100 or more registry entries because the files referred to 
cannot be removed (even with industrial strength unlockers applied to 
them...)

BOTTOM LINE:

1. Use a free trial and see how it works on your system. If they don't have 
a free trial, don't entertain buying or using it.
2. Before installing any of these products make sure you have a system 
restore point and, ideally, a partition image of your boot drive.
3. Be prepared to pay for full protection; free is nice, but it isn't enough 
if you spend a lot of time connected to networks.

What did I end up with?

AVG (paid version). It was the only one that uninstalled sweetly and 
cleanly, had all of the functionality I require, and is unobtrusive, does 
not slow the booting and running of the system excessively, and is stable. 
It is not the fastest, and it is not the cheapest, but it meets my 
requirements.

(This is NOT a general endorsement; as noted above, results on YOUR system 
may be completely different...)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Sample COBOL Copybook and Data Files for Testing

- **From:** Graham Hobbs <ghobbs@cdpwise.net>
- **Date:** 2013-01-25T07:55:33-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<0qv4g81gojgbaalmn59icn566hu6iken57@4ax.com>`

```
On Thu, 24 Jan 2013 12:44:54 -0800 (PST), paulsalimmansour@gmail.com
wrote:

>Hi,
>
>I'm looking for some sample or public COBOL copybook and associated data files so that I can test some code for converting COBOL data types. I've googled far and wide and can't find anything. Any help would be greatly appreciated.
>

---
Bits from here you may be able to use ..
           EXEC SQL DECLARE ALLTYP1 TABLE
                  "ABIGINT" BIGINT not null,
                  "ABLOB" BLOB(40) NOT LOGGED NOT COMPACT ,
                  "ACHAR" CHAR(10) NOT NULL,
                  "ACLOB" CLOB(41) NOT LOGGED NOT COMPACT ,
                  "ADATE" DATE ,
                  "ADECIMAL" DECIMAL(3,1) ,
                  "ADOUBLE" DOUBLE ,
                  "AINTEGER" INTEGER ,
                  "ALONGVARCHAR" LONG VARCHAR,
                  "AREAL" REAL ,
                  "ASMALLINT" SMALLINT ,
                  "ATIME" TIME ,
                  "ATIMESTAMP" TIMESTAMP ,
                  "AVARCHAR" VARCHAR(10) )
           END-EXEC.

       01 ALLTYP1.
         05 ABIGINT PIC S9(18) COMP-5.
         05 ABLOB USAGE IS SQL TYPE IS BLOB(40).
         05 ACHAR PIC X(10).
         05 ACLOB USAGE IS SQL TYPE IS CLOB(41).
         05 ADATE PIC X(10).
         05 ADECIMAL PIC S9(2)V9(1) COMP-3.
         05 ADOUBLE USAGE IS COMP-2.
         05 AINTEGER PIC S9(9) COMP-5.
         05 ALONGVARCHAR.
           49 LEN PIC S9(4) COMP-5.
           49 DAT PIC X(00087).
         05 AREAL USAGE IS COMP-1.
         05 ASMALLINT PIC S9(4) COMP-5.
         05 ATIME PIC X(8).
         05 ATIMESTAMP PIC X(26).
         05 AVARCHAR.
           49 LEN1 PIC S9(4) COMP-5.
           49 DAT1 PIC X(10).
..
CONNECT RESET;
CONNECT TO WCB3;
DROP   TABLE ALLTYP1;
CREATE TABLE ALLTYP1 (
       ABIGINT                        BIGINT NOT NULL,
       ACHAR                          CHAR(10) NOT NULL,
       ADATE                          DATE,
       ADECIMAL                       DECIMAL(3,1),
       ADOUBLE                        DOUBLE,
       AINTEGER                       INTEGER,
       AREAL                          REAL,
       ASMALLINT                      SMALLINT,
       ATIME                          TIME,
       ATIMESTAMP                     TIMESTAMP,
PRIMARY KEY (
             ABIGINT
            )
       );
INSERT INTO ALLTYP1 (
       ABIGINT,
       ACHAR,
       ADATE,
       ADECIMAL,
       ADOUBLE,
       AINTEGER,
       AREAL,
       ASMALLINT,
       ATIME,
       ATIMESTAMP
       )
 VALUES(
       01.00,
       'BCDEFGHIJK',
       CURRENT DATE,
       94.4,
       000010107,
       1000157,
       1000307,
       10155,
       CURRENT TIME,
       CURRENT TIMESTAMP
       );
INSERT INTO ALLTYP1 (
       ABIGINT,
       ACHAR,
       ADATE,
       ADECIMAL,
       ADOUBLE,
       AINTEGER,
       AREAL,
       ASMALLINT,
       ATIME,
       ATIMESTAMP
       )
 VALUES(
       60.00,
       'KLMNOPQRST',
       CURRENT DATE,
       81.1,
       000016125,
       1017857,
       1018007,
       19005,
       CURRENT TIME,
       CURRENT TIMESTAMP
       );
CONNECT RESET;
..
the INSERT's don't create all the datatypes. For one of my apps I
generate sixty test rows; for posting here there's only two and
doesn't include all the datatypes and no, I don't have a flat file.
Graham Hobbs
---

## Thread: OT: It's all about perception... — 1 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: It's all about perception...

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2013-01-27T11:50:55+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<amj4uhFbp0vU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
http://sunlive.co.nz/news/38135-world-first-at-tauranga-hospital.html

If I was sub-editor at a newspaper (in another lifetime I worked as a 
photo-journalist on a newspaper and had close experience with this ilk), I 
could make the following headlines for this item, and they would all be 
equally "valid"....:

1. My paper has an anti-American bias.

"Kiwi guinea pigs used to test new US technology."

2. My paper is neutral about the USA.

"New US technology may bring benefits for Kiwis."

3. My paper loves the USA and the Editor is an American.

"US surgical breakthrough is shared with NZ"

My point is that the perception of sub-editors can have a big impact on how 
people read the news.

As far as the case in point, as someone moving into the zone where I might 
require such surgery, I'm very pleased we can participate and it is 
reassuring to know it is right on the doorstep... :-)

Pete.
```
